# Cybersecurity Foundations

> Set a rock-solid foundation for your network, users, and data by learning about the basics of cybersecurity. Join security expert Malcolm Shore as he describes how to assess and mitigate risks using various cybersecurity frameworks and control standards, such as NIST and COBIT.
 
Malcolm details pertinent cyber threats and how they operate, including how cyber criminals hide their attacks, how adv

### Introduction

#### Understanding the cybersecurity frameworks and standards
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cybersecurity-frameworks-and-standards?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cybersecurity-frameworks-and-standards?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Malcolm] The internet is an essential service as necessary as electricity and other utilities.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cybersecurity-frameworks-and-standards?u=76281980&t=6)** That makes cybersecurity a requirement for everyone.
>

> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cybersecurity-frameworks-and-standards?u=76281980&t=12)** I've spent a career helping governments and businesses protect their systems against cyber attack.

#### What you should know
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/what-you-should-know?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Host] We're all fairly familiar with the use of computers and the internet of the risks of viruses and other forms of malware and of the basics of avoiding such attacks.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=10)** However, over the years, cybersecurity professionals have developed a number of standards and frameworks, which together provide a structured approach to managing the cyber risk.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=22)** Understanding these frameworks provides a good foundation for anyone with an interest in or wishing to build a career in cybersecurity.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=31)** This LinkedIn Learning Course is suitable for anyone who wishes to learn about cybersecurity.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=36)** You don't need to be a security professional to take it.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=39)** It would help to be familiar with the following two basic frameworks.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=45)** ISACA's control objectives for IT, provide the best practice model of IT processes and help provide the context for the threats to and protection of IT systems.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=57)** Nist cybersecurity framework provides the most recognized approach to cybersecurity an internet enabled world and some pre-reading would be helpful in taking this course.

### 1. Cyber Threats

#### Understanding the Cyber Kill Chain
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] In the 1990s, cyber attack was generally associated with pranks by bored teenagers just hacking around for fun.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=9)** However, the potential for committing crime via the Internet didn't go unnoticed, nor did the possibility of exploiting connectivity for intelligence gathering.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=20)** Nowadays, cyber attacks come mostly from organized criminals and state-sponsored agents using well-defined end-to-end business processes.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=31)** In 2009, a team from the *Lockheed Martin Cyber Emergency Response Team produced a seminal paper on cyber attack called "Intelligence-Driven Computer Network Defense Informed by Analysis of Adversary Campaigns and Intrusion Kill Chains."
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=49)** This can be downloaded from their website shown here.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=53)** The research paper introduced the concept of what is now commonly known as the Cyber Kill Chain.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=60)** The Cyber Kill Chain views an attack in seven stages: Reconnaissance, Weaponization, Delivery, Exploitation, Installation, Command and Control, and Action.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=74)** An attack doesn't always progress from one step to the next.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=77)** They'll often overlap, but each stage represents a milestone in prosecuting the attack.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=84)** Reconnaissance is the term given to finding a target and understanding its characteristics, the cyber equivalent of casing the joint.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=93)** Individuals typically have one address on the Internet, which has been allocated by their Internet Service Provider, whereas a business may have a number of addresses in what's known as their Internet Domain.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=106)** A cyber attack against a business target will start with a known website address, and then scan the Internet space around that address for other systems used by the target.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=118)** The business will see this as a response check on every host in its domain.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=122)** This is known as an IP address scan.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=126)** When the attacker has a list of active hosts, he or she will scan each host in turn to find out what entry points are exposed.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=135)** This is known as a Port scan.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=137)** This is done to identify potential vectors for attack and check the versions of software used in those vectors.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=146)** Attacks nowadays are not done manually.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=148)** An attacker will usually purchase time on a network of compromised computers in order to run automated scans.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=156)** These networks are known as Botnets, and may consist of hundreds of thousands, if not millions of compromised computers.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=165)** This allows cyber attacks to be run at scale.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=169)** Weaponization means taking a known vulnerability and customizing it to a specific target or group of targets, and integrating it to enable it to be run from an automated cyber attack platform.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=183)** The weaponized malware may be designed to exploit a vulnerability in a specific version of an operating system, or target a specific online banking website.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=195)** In the age of hacking as a business, cyber criminals will often purchase the weaponized malware from dedicated developers, rather than develop their own.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=206)** The most common way of delivering malware is to attach an infected document, a PDF image, or other electronic item in a way that when the document is opened, the malware will self-install.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=220)** This file can then be sent to the victim via email, a process known as phishing.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=225)** Another way might be to find a vulnerable website, infect it with malware, and send an email invitation to the target to visit the website.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=236)** If the victim visits the website, then the malware is downloaded and infects their workstation.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=243)** A third way might be to use default user IDs and passwords built into software on the target system, or to use a stolen user ID and password to enter the target system and directly implant the malware.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=258)** It's also possible to find flaws in the software that's exposed to the Internet and to manually deliver the malware.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=265)** In practice, an attack will often require establishing a beachhead on an Internet-exposed host, and then using that to penetrate deeper into the system to get to the real target, which may not be directly connected to the Internet.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=280)** Finally, an infected flash drive can be used to deliver the malware, and this can be very effective if the target system is not connected to the Internet.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=289)** This requires that a user of the target system can be persuaded or tricked into using the flash drive.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=297)** For email attachment and flash drive attacks, the infected item will exploit a vulnerability in the target software post-delivery, when the document is opened.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=307)** A compromised website may similarly download HTML code, which takes advantage of a browser vulnerability.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=315)** In the case of remote access, the exploitation phase may use a packet stream to exploit a vulnerability in the protocol of an Internet-exposed service, or may simply use cracked or stolen credentials.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=330)** After the exploitation phase, the malware or intruder may simply take action, skipping directly to the last phase of the Cyber Kill Chain.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=339)** However, the more usual case is that a payload is installed either into the memory, or onto the hard disk of the target system.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=347)** Additionally, some form of mechanism may be introduced to make sure the payload is restarted every time the system is rebooted.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=356)** One way of doing this in Windows is to add a registry entry to automatically run the payload when the system starts up.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=363)** The payload will often be, or include, a means of maintaining ongoing access into a command shell.
>
> **[6:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=371)** A system compromise is often automated.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=374)** Once a payload has been installed, the first action it takes will be to connect back to a Command and Control server to register as a compromised host.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=384)** The attacker will then want to direct the implant to take action, such as listing the sub (indistinct) files, extracting specific named files, modifying or replacing software, and so on.
>
> **[6:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=397)** An important feature of the payload is that it can determine the address of the Command and Control server, which may change over time.
>
> **[6:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=405)** Exactly what form of action is carried out by the payload when it arrives at its target depends upon the motives of the attacker.
>
> **[6:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=414)** A hactivist may want to deface a website.
>
> **[6:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=417)** A state-sponsored agent may want to steal sensitive information, and a cyber criminal may want to access a bank account in order to steal money.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=428)** The common theme, however, is that whatever the action is, it's unlikely to be in the best interests of the target.
>
> **[7:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=436)** Stop for a moment and think about this week's current events.
>
> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=440)** Have you heard about a recent attack?
>
> **[7:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=443)** How might you relate what you've heard to the Cyber Kill Chain?
>
> **[7:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=447)** You probably heard about the action that happened, but not about the delivery phase.
>
> **[7:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-cyber-kill-chain?u=76281980&t=453)** How might that have occurred?

#### Pre-cyber threats
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the early days of computing, the security threats faced by businesses reflected traditional pre-IT fraud.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=8)** One traditional method of fraud is to have non-existence employees on the payroll with pay being drawn and put into someone else's account.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=19)** In January, 2012, a woman in Hawaii was indicted for allegedly attempting to embezzle money from the security guarding firm for which she worked by registering and taking the pay for two fictional employees.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=35)** The scam would net her more than $200,000.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=40)** A second method is known as salami fraud, so named because it resembles shaving a thin slice of meat.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=48)** In this case, what's shaved is the fractions taken off in a rounding calculation, or changing transactions by taking a few cents from them.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=57)** If the business deals in millions of transactions, then this can become a significant fraud.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=64)** A third form of fraud is payment for non-existent goods.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=68)** This can happen when one person has the ability to raise a purchase order, receipt goods, and issue checks.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=78)** A similar problem occurs for individuals when an online seller receives money in advance for non-existent goods, or has the auctions rigged by entering false bids to inflate prices.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=92)** As the use of computer systems grew, so did the threats.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=96)** More sophisticated forms of fraud emerged, taking advantage, in many cases, of the weakening controls in the IT environment.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=106)** The early days of computing brought with them youngsters enthralled by the challenge of using a computer and a modem to break into other computers.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=115)** And so began the age of the teen hacker.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=118)** This was mostly individual challenge and peer recognition, although there were some early instances of what we now know as cyber espionage and cyber crime.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=130)** The classic hacker of the 1990s was Kevin Mitnick, otherwise known as Condor.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=136)** After a decade of hacking for no other reason than to demonstrate how good he was, Mitnick was finally caught and sentenced to three years jail time.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=146)** The full story, book and film, is described on the "Takedown" website.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=152)** As the use of the web grew and information websites and business web portals became more common, we saw bored teenagers defacing websites and leaving I-got-you messages.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=165)** Website defacements were also targeted as part of politically-motivated attacks and used to communicate a political message in what's known as hacktivism.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=176)** As dependence upon IT systems grew, another security threat to emerge was the denial-of-service attack, in which a remote attacker can compromise IT systems through exploiting vulnerabilities or through overwhelming their ability to handle the size of information flows.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=196)** As the internet grew, adversaries were able to take control of large numbers of computers, known as a botnet, and focus them on a single target, substantially amplifying the impact of the denial of service.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=210)** This is known as a distributed denial of service, or DDoS.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=216)** A global example of a denial of service occurred in February, 2014 when an unknown attacker launched a rolling wave of distributed denial-of-service attacks on a variety of targets, country by country, around the world.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=231)** The scale of the attack was enormous.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=233)** The attack used a special feature of the network time protocol to amplify the data.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=239)** By the time the packet had reached the target, it had been amplified 50 times, making this the equivalent of about 250,000 individual denial-of-service attacks.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=252)** One of the businesses targeted in this campaign was attacked by 4,278 individual IP addresses from over 80 countries, delivering a continuous stream of over 1 million packets per minute for about an hour.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/pre-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=268)** The graphic shows the timeline of data arriving from the internet to this company.

#### The emergence of the cyber threats
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] By the late 1990s, electronic commerce had become a significant part of the economy and organized crime was starting to look at the potential for low risk, high gain crimes through the internet.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=13)** Cybercrime started to grow rapidly, with one of the major targets being access to databases of credit card information.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=21)** The loss of credit cards known as a data breach became a significant risk for businesses as the payment card industry introduced penalties for non-compliance with their cybersecurity standards.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=33)** By 2010, cybercrime had become as big an industry as illicit drugs.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=41)** There have been many data breaches over the last few years.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=45)** This visualization of data breaches shows the massive breaches in the Shanghai Police, Syniverse and Facebook.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=52)** With a breach of half a billion records, Facebook takes top place in the last year or so.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=59)** Credit cards are not the only sensitive information that can be breached.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=64)** In June 2015, the US government admitted that intruders had stolen personnel files, including security clearance data of over 4 million current and former government employees.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=77)** Governments have, for some time, been the victims of hacking attacks but with little evidence to be able to positively attribute the source.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=85)** In June 2007, US officials disclosed that hackers broke into the Pentagon through a directed attack on elements of the email system and called it the most successful cyber attack at that time on the US Defense Department.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=103)** US attributed the attack to China, one of a number of Chinese attacks on Western governments known by the code name Titan Rain.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=112)** China denied any involvement in the attacks.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=115)** In 2010, the first attributed cyber sabotage case was made public.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=122)** The US confirmed that it had worked with Israel to develop and deploy the Stuxnet malware to attack the Iranian nuclear enrichment program.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=131)** It was successful, disrupting the nuclear production capability of Iran.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=136)** We've seen a new form of attack of recent times, cyber influence operations, with the Clinton email attack which focused on election meddling.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=146)** Take a moment to check out the world's largest data breaches.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-emergence-of-the-cyber-threats?u=76281980&t=155)** Take a look at some of the smaller attacks and see if you can find how many records were lost by the craft beer company BrewDog.

#### Botnets and the cybercrime industry
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] By its very nature, cybercrime lends itself to automation.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=5)** The malware writers in the cybercrime business ecosystem, are now some of the most proficient software writers in the world.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=13)** But to make money from cybercrime requires automation to carry out attacks at scale.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=20)** In the early days of computers, viruses propagated through floppy disks.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=25)** As the internet grew, infections started to appear in file downloads, and as floppy drives phased out, USB drives took their place as a vector for infection.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=38)** Compromised websites began to host malware, and take advantage of browser weaknesses to infect visitors.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=47)** As the impact of viruses moved from nuisance value to financial gain, so did automation of the crime.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=53)** By automating and taking advantage of the growing popularity of email, and the sheer size of the internet, organized crime was able to achieve cybercrime at a scale which is now eclipsed illicit drug dealing.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=66)** Cybercrime scales by what's known as a botnet.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=72)** A botnet consists of a criminal, known as the Botmaster, who runs a number of command and control systems.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=79)** The botmaster will usually encrypt his or her command and control access, and often disguise it to look like normal web traffic when accessing these systems.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=90)** Legitimate websites are compromised and used for command and control, operating only for a certain period of time before being discarded, and a new one taking over.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=102)** The botmaster runs the command and control servers, and each command and control system, in turn, controls a large number of computers known as zombies that have been infected with a back door.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=115)** A large botnet may have over millions zombies under its control.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=120)** A typical task for the zombie would be to extract files from a target, to use the targets as a source of email spam, or to send specially crafted packets out as part of a distributed denial of service attack.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=135)** Given that the command and control servers change, both the botmaster and the zombies have to be able to find the current server, and they do this by using a domain generation algorithm.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=146)** This allows the malware to predict what URLs and/or IP addresses may be active at any particular time in the future.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=155)** The domain name may stay the same, and the IP address change, or the domain and IP address may change together.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=163)** The most notorious botnet, and the grandfather of many subsequent variants is Zeus.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=169)** Zeus itself is a botnet construction kit which enables an attacker to create a customized Zeus-style botnet through a simple-to-use desktop application.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=182)** The construction kit delivers a selectable remote access trojan or RAT, and a command and control module.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=190)** The Zeus source code was leaked in 2010, and as a result it's been used by many cyber criminals to carry out attacks.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=198)** It's also been used as the foundation to add functionality and create new botnets, such as Citadel, ICE 9 and Gameover Zeus.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=209)** Zeus's main purpose is to steal online credentials.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=213)** It includes automated features such as copying the protected storage area which contains internet passwords, intercepting account credentials typed into a browser, or even modifying banking webpages sent from a server to add requests for passwords.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=231)** Zeus can be used to target both computers and smartphones.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=236)** Smartphone infections allows Zeus to steal banking access codes that are sent via text message.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=243)** Cybercrime is a complex, highly-organized business, involving organized criminals, a variety of service enablers, malware producers, and of course victims.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=256)** The criminal organizations decide on the crime campaigns, and are supported by the service enablers who run the systems needed to execute the campaign.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=266)** Banking fraud campaign, for example, will follow the cyber kill chain.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=270)** It will select targets, through surveillance, which are vulnerable to a specific technical attack.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=277)** The malware developers will create malware specifically targeted to this attack, customizing it perhaps to a specific bank website.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=286)** A team of testers will quality assure the software through testing. The malicious payload is delivered, and installed through the botnet.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=296)** The criminals will often not have their own botnet, but will rent one from another criminal group.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=302)** For those attacks that are successful, the stolen funds are then transferred to the disposable bank account set up for the campaign.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=310)** The aim then is to withdraw the funds as cash, to break the electronic money trail.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=316)** To do this, the campaign use what known as Money Mules.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=320)** The often poor unfortunates, who were recruited to go and collect the money by withdrawing it out of the fraudster accounts, with the very real risk of being caught.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=331)** While money mules have traditionally been small time criminals or people with financial difficulties, an interesting development is the emergence of the professional mules for hire, who for about 40% of the money collected, offer a fast and responsive service.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=349)** These services exist in most US cities.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/botnets-and-the-cybercrime-industry?u=76281980&t=352)** Once such service has been reported as moving between 30,000 and a hundred thousand dollars per day.

#### Cloaking and alternate data streams
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Attackers who penetrate systems with malware go out of their way to hide it once it's on the target system.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=7)** If the infection can evade detection, it's more likely to accomplish its intended goals.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=13)** Let's have a look at some of the ways in which malware can hide.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=18)** The first method is to use the techniques used by the Windows operating system to hide its own activities.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=25)** An example of this is the hidden history folder.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=29)** I'm in a command shell and I'll go into a folder in my user applications directory by typing cd appdata \local \microsoft\windows.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=46)** When I list the contents of the directory, there's a number of files and folders, but there's no history sub-directory.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=54)** I can list hidden files with dir /ah, but there's still no history sub-directory.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=63)** However, things are not always as they seem.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=66)** The history sub-directory does exist, but we just can't see it.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=71)** Let's try to change directories and go into it.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=75)** cd history.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=78)** Well that worked.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=79)** So now let's see what's here.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=83)** And we see the file desktop.ini.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=87)** This is the method Microsoft uses to hide the sub-directory.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=91)** When I take a look at what's in it, by typing desktop.ini I see it has two cloaking entries.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=102)** The first is a CLSID line, which stops the sub-directory from being included in file-based finds.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=110)** And the second, the UICLSID line, which stops the sub-directory from being seen using Windows Explorer.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=123)** Another little known way of hiding on disk is to use what's known as alternate data streams.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=128)** In the early MS-DOS and FAT file systems, files were simply strings of data which could be read byte by byte by applications.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=138)** In NTFS, a file is a complex structure.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=142)** NTF files contain as a minimum a section called $DATA, which is where the data read by an application resides.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=150)** This is the data stream.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=151)** However, a file may have many other sections, each with its own name, and each of which can hold information.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=159)** These are called alternate data streams.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=163)** Importantly, windows only recognizes the $DATA section so data in any alternate data streams isn't generally recognized.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=173)** Okay, back at the terminal, let's go into the temporary folder and create a new file called datafile.txt by typing, type con, the console, to datafile.txt.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=193)** Here's a text file which has nothing much to hide.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=202)** It's simply a string of words that is saved to disk.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=216)** Okay, that's created the file, let's check it.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=219)** Type datafile.txt, and we can see the contents as we entered them.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=226)** We can also check its size.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=230)** Dir datafile.txt is 105 bytes long.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=235)** I'll create another file called adsfile.txt.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=241)** Type con: to adsfile.txt.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=247)** This is my secret message which I want to store where no one can find it.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=265)** Now I'll insert that into a hidden data stream in datafile.txt by typing, type adsfile.txt to datafile.txt, colon, hidden.txt.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=284)** Let's see what datafile.txt looks like now.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=289)** type datafile.txt and dir.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=297)** So there's no apparent change.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=300)** However, if I now type more from datafile.txt colon hidden.txt we see the hidden text.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=317)** Alternate data streams can also be used to hide executable files.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=321)** As an example, I'll insert the Windows calculator into this text file.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=328)** type \windows \system32 \calc.exe to datafile.txt and we'll call it mycalc.exe type datafile.txt And dir, and again, we see no change.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=357)** We can use a special form of the Windows instrumentation tool, wmic, to run this hidden executable.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=365)** wmic process call create '"C:\temp \datafile.txt: mycalc.exe" Then we have the calculator executed.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=392)** While alternate data streams can't be seen in Explorer or by using the dir command normally, it is possible to use the /r command line option on the dir command to see them.
>
> **[6:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=404)** dir datafile.txt /r Now we can see that this file does have two additional streams.
>
> **[6:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cloaking-and-alternate-data-streams?u=76281980&t=415)** hidden.txt and mycalc.exe.

#### Hiding using processes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Speaker] Let's now look at how we build a program which uses a more sophisticated means of hiding by looking at a simple malware function written as a Windows process to intercept all keystrokes.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=12)** In Microsoft, when a key on the keyboard is pressed, an event is signaled to the operating system.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=20)** Windows uses its keyboard driver to read the character that's been pressed and sends it as a message to the application that's waiting for it.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=29)** However, Windows also allows other processors to look at the message as it passes through the system.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=35)** That's how hotkeys work.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=37)** Windows does this by something known as a keyboard hook callback routine.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=42)** It hooks the key that's been pressed and then calls back after processing it to pass it onto its original destination.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=50)** This technique can be used to write what's known as a simple keystroke logger.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=55)** This only takes a few lines of code but it's quite powerful.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=59)** I've prepared a keyboard hook program called wmisvc64.cpp, which we can see here.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=67)** This is a very simple program.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=69)** The first five lines provide the standard setup code for a C++ program.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=75)** These are followed at line seven by code which is executed when the program first starts to open an output file called intercept.txt that we'll use later to store intercepted characters.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=88)** The next six lines specify a callback procedure.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=91)** This is the code that's executed when a key is pressed.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=95)** Events in Windows are quite granular.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=98)** For instance, a key press involves two events, a key down and a key up.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=103)** We only need to check one of these, so the callback routine at line 11 just checks for the WM Key Up event.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=111)** When it sees this, it writes out to the output file the data associated with this event which is the internal Windows code for the character pressed on the keyboard.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=122)** The final action in this routine is to pass on the event by calling the next hook in the chain, using the aptly named CallNextHookEx function, which will allow the message to resume its path to the target application.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=138)** The remainder of the program is the main controlling logic for the Windows process that supports the callback routine.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=145)** We don't need to go into this in detail, but do note at line 20 that we're registering a HotKey with MOD.ALT and zero times three nine which is program speak for ALT nine.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=157)** When this is pressed, the program will terminate.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=161)** Okay, let's see this program in action.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=164)** We can compile this at the command line by entering cl wmisvc64.cpp.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=176)** Okay, that's compiled and we can now run it by typing wmisvc64 and we'll now close the command window.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=190)** Let's do a bit of simple forensics and look at what's running on our computer.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=195)** We'll type control Alt del, open the task manager and look at the processes.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=205)** There's nothing in the application list to show the interceptors running.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=209)** If we scroll down to the background processes, at the very bottom, we can see wmisvc64.exe.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=220)** There's little about it to distinguish it from the normal Windows system processes which are running.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=226)** It's in plain sight, but it's fairly well hidden, nevertheless.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=231)** Let's type some data into a notepad document.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=235)** This is my secret note on writing callback routines to capture keystrokes.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=253)** Okay, I'll close this.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=256)** We won't save it, and I'll press ALT nine to terminate the intercept.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=264)** Let's use Explorer to check the log file the Intercept program has been using.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=270)** And here we see what the program has intercepted.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=273)** The intercept contains our exit and also the note that we wrote.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=278)** And it also contains other keyboard activities, such as ALT and shift keys, which displays various special characters.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=287)** Of course, what an attacker would like to see in the Intercept they capture is our system access and banking account login.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hiding-using-processes?u=76281980&t=296)** Lesson here is that if an adversary can get access to implant malware on a computer, even a simple program of a few lines and running as a normal user, it can be difficult to detect and has the ability to read everything that's typed including access codes and passwords.

#### Controlling the target through a rootkit
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Malware using the basic hiding and cloaking techniques can be detected by a knowledgeable investigator.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=7)** Consequently, the more sophisticated attackers have developed techniques which install malware, not just as an application or process, but deep into the heart of the operating system.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=19)** This kind of malware is known as a rootkit.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=23)** In order to deploy a rootkit, an attacker must first penetrate the target system and then use what's known as a dropper to install the rootkit, which it either carries as a payload or subsequently downloads.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=37)** The job of the dropper is to check whether the rootkit already exists on the system, whether the system is operating inside a virtual machine, and special checks such as the country in which it's operating.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=51)** Once satisfied that this is a legitimate and available target, it inserts the rootkit into the system and makes sure it can restart after a system boot and starts it running.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=63)** A rootkit doesn't exploit your vulnerabilities.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=66)** It's designed to hide, operate, and carry out its mission using normal system functions.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=71)** Similarly, a rootkit isn't a virus.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=75)** It's an implant which exists in a target, but it doesn't by itself propagate.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=80)** It can however, be combined with virus-like code to enable propagation laterally once installed in a system.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=89)** A rootkit is designed to bypass intrusion detection systems.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=93)** For example, it may contain code to look for and disable certain forms of antivirus or host intrusion detection software.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=102)** It will also want to avoid detection by a forensics analyst and the best place to hide is in the operating system kernel.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=110)** This is the inner core of the operating system.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=113)** An intrusion detection systems can't easily see inside the kernel.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=118)** Getting into the kernel requires rootkits to be coded as a special form of program called a loadable kernel module or driver.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=128)** Microsoft provides the Windows driver development kit, or DDK, for developing loadable kernel modules.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=136)** A driver uses quite complex coding techniques so I'll not delve into the process of developing and deploying a driver.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=143)** Suffice to say, drivers operate in the deepest part of the kernel, what's known as ring zero, and this gives them access to all the kernel data structures.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=153)** In addition to being in the kernel, rootkits run with elevated privileges.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=159)** There's very little that a rootkit can't do and it can be very hard to find.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=164)** Being a privileged process in the kernel allows a rootkit to employ direct kernel object modification, or DKOM.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=173)** The kernel uses data structures to keep track of its environment.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=177)** An example is the process data structure, held in the EPROCESS module.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=183)** This is a doubly linked list, meaning each entry has two pointers.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=189)** The forward pointer chain starts at the head of the list and each entry points to the next entry in the list.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=197)** The backward points chain starts at the end of the list and each entry points to the previous one.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=204)** Using these pointers, the kernel can keep track of and manage all active processes.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=211)** When a user opens task manager, what actually happens is that the task manager application loads and then calls the kernel asking for a list of applications and processes.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=222)** The kernel checks its data structures, creates the lists and then sends them back to the task manager for display.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=230)** When the rootkit is loaded, one of the first things it does is to examine the EPROCESS list.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=236)** It follows the forward chain to find its own process and the previous and next ones.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=242)** Once it has them, it can then change the pointers so that the previous process points to the one following the rootkit and the one following has its backward pointer changed to point to the one before the rootkit.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=255)** The first known rootkit to perform DKOM appeared around 2006 and did just this.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=262)** Changing the list pointers in the EPROCESS data structure to point around its own entry.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=268)** It then hides rootkit activity from the task manager and event scheduler.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=274)** DKOM can be used on other data structures such as the driver list and the list of open ports.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=279)** Rootkits will also intercept and remove their entries from directory and file lists.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=286)** A common way to provide remote command and control is to create an encrypted channel through the use of secure shell connections.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=295)** This gives an attacker remote control over compromised systems, and at the same time encrypts any malware that may be downloaded to prevent it from being detected by network-based intrusion detection systems and monitoring tools.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=309)** Using SSH has the advantage of requiring a username and password to be entered, thereby ensuring only the adversary can use the back door to access the rootkit.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=321)** Trojan Downloader 3, or TDL3, is the third generation of rootkit developed by the Dogma Millions cybercrime group and is an example of a real world rootkit.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=332)** The rootkit adds itself as a printer driver which gives it kernel mode driver privileges.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=338)** It installs an encrypted file system for its own use that begins at the end of the hard disk and grows backwards to the beginning of the disk.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=347)** Windows just thinks this is free space with nothing in it which means it's not detected by traditional scanning techniques.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=354)** TDL3 operates on a per install cost model by recording when it gets installed on a target and who purchased this source version.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/controlling-the-target-through-a-rootkit?u=76281980&t=364)** The TDL3 rootkit is used to download, install, and hide malicious payload modules that can then do keystroke monitoring, carry out distributed denial of service attacks, and many more actions.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** dkom (3), eprocess (3), tdl3 (3), ddk (1), ssh (1)
> **Definitions:** known as (2), is a  (2), is an  (1)
> **Prerequisites:** install (4)
> **CLI Commands:** find (2), ssh (1)
> **Code Keywords:** module (2), this. (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), such as (1)
