#### Phishing and watering holes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As the security of the operating application surface improved, direct penetration of a target became more difficult.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=8)** Consequently, cyber criminals looked for other ways to get malware into their target.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=14)** On a workstation, the weakest link, of course, is the person using the computer, and so we become the target.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=21)** The first approach taken by attackers to exploit the user is what's called phishing.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=26)** This involves sending an email with a malicious attachment or a link to a malicious site to a lot of users, hoping that at least one will take the bait and open the attachment or click on the link.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=39)** At this point, the malware downloads into the target system and begins executing.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=46)** A phishing email will do as much as possible to entice its recipient to open its malicious attachment or to click on the link.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=54)** In the early days, this might have been a rather crude appeal to greed by suggesting the recipient had won a lottery they hadn't entered.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=62)** But nowadays, the better phishing attacks are much more sophisticated.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=66)** The email may pretend to have an up-to-date analysis of a current news topic.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=71)** It may look like an official bank email asking you, ironically, to check your security or account settings.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=78)** It may look like a postal email advising you a parcel is ready for pickup.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=84)** Attachments are always suspicious, and hovering over a supposed government hyperlink to find it links to [GF65mmjy.com](https://GF65mmjy.com) is a sure giveaway.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=98)** Sometimes phishing attacks aren't carried out by sending email to a large recipient list but are designed to trap a specific person.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=106)** These are referred to as spear phishing emails.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=110)** In this case, the attacker will have spent a fair bit of time researching the target and will craft an email which may purport to come from a colleague inside the business and use common business terminology.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=124)** These are not necessarily harder to detect as phishing emails, but they are designed to have the recipient let down their guard.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=133)** Consider your own email over the last week or month.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=137)** Have you received a phishing email?
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=139)** What did you do?
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=140)** Did you open it or just delete it?
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=143)** Was it fairly crude or did it look quite convincing?
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=147)** Many phishing emails these days can be quite sophisticated and difficult to detect as traps.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=154)** Some special forms of phishing campaigns have been seen.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=158)** Some phishing campaigns target mobile users while others target users of voice over IP services, an attack also known as phishing.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=170)** Phishing attacks, like any cyber attack, can be costly.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=174)** Between 2013 and 2015, cyber attackers scammed over a hundred million dollars out of Facebook and Google by asking for it through phishing emails.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=188)** Waterholes are another type of attack focused on the user.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=192)** In this attack, a website which focuses on a specific set of users, doctors, for instance, is compromised.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=200)** The site is typically one commonly used by the group and when they subsequently visit it, their malware is downloaded.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=209)** The attacker hopes that the user will do this on their business computer, hence enabling access to their organization.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=217)** In 2020, Kaspersky discovered a waterhole attack targeting religious charities.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=223)** The campaign was named Holy Water and worked by tricking visitors into downloading an Adobe update which contained the malware.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=233)** The attackers have yet to be identified.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=237)** A good example of how an event can trigger phishing attacks is the 2022 FIFA World Cup where phishing attacks targeting the Middle East doubled in the lead up to the event.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=249)** Many of the emails look like they were from the FIFA help desk, as we can see here.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=254)** The goal of these phishing attacks included financial fraud, gaining credentials, stealing information, and surveillance.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/phishing-and-watering-holes?u=76281980&t=263)** Many of the emails focused on betting on the World Cup enabling, the attackers to gain credentials which they could reuse.

#### Understanding advanced persistent threats
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Over recent years, there's been an increasing recognition of the threat posed by nation states using highly sophisticated malware known as advanced persistent threats, or APTs.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=12)** This is malware directed at political and military targets using a multiple vectors to attack.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=19)** APTs have a high degree of stealthiness and can persist over a long period of time.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=26)** There are five key characteristics which make APTs quite different to rootkits.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=30)** The first is that they tend to be highly customized to a specific target or set of targets rather than being a common code module.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=40)** An associated characteristic is that they're focused on targeting the specific system or set of systems for which they've been designed, rather than being opportunistic.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=50)** They usually have multiple advanced and often zero-day exploits through which to exploit the target.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=57)** Their deployment is likely to be controlled or have some level of intervention by humans rather than being fully automated.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=65)** And once in place, operate in a low and slow manner in order to remain stealthy and unnoticed.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=74)** An APT may have one or more objectives depending upon the source of the attack, and these may change over time.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=81)** An APT may be sent by an adversary to carry out espionage against nation-state targets with the intention of stealing sensitive information.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=92)** It may be sent to cause sabotage by disrupting the operation of the critical infrastructure systems such as telecommunications, power, and water.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=103)** An APT must infiltrate its target, find a place to hide, and then continue to operate if it's to succeed as a persistent threat.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=114)** This requires it to have five key functions.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=116)** The first is command and control, the ability for the remote attacker to direct tasking and configuration of the implanted malware, to download new payloads, and to provide malware updates.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=132)** This requires the APT to connect back to its command and control server to look for tasking or to open an access path for the adversary to gain direct control.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=143)** The more sophisticated APTs don't operate as discreet applications, but attach themselves to an existing application or process that's running in memory.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=153)** This is known as malware injection.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=157)** An APT wants to remain invisible for as long as possible and operate as a low-and-slow attack, stealthily extracting what it needs with as little impact on the host computer and without generating regular or predictable network traffic.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=172)** Consequently, a substantial amount of effort is invested in the cloaking subsystem to ensure that malicious actions can't be observed by legitimate operators of the systems.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=184)** APT software is typically designed to collect information and it needs to send it back to its control server.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=192)** This is known as exfiltration, and a good exfiltration system will not only encrypt the information being sent so that it isn't seen by any monitoring systems, but it may also hide it in the kind of packets that are normally ignored, such as HTTP or DNS requests.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=211)** The final function is known as reignition.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=214)** In order to remain operational for a period of time, an APT needs to restart when the system is rebooted or if the system administrator attempts to remove it.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=224)** The basic approach to reignition on a Windows system is to write a new entry into the registry to instruct Windows to run the malware loader.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=234)** This may not be the only reignition mechanism, however, as often, an APT will use multiple means of reigniting.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=242)** So what does an APT really look like when it's militarized and deployed by a state?
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=249)** While a malware module called Agent.BTZ was the earliest recorded APT, infecting the Pentagon in 2008, the most notorious military-grade APT to date has been Stuxnet, detected in 2010.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=266)** Stuxnet was designed specifically to target centrifuges in the Iranian nuclear program, targeting the Siemens industrial plant equipment used in nuclear fuel enrichment, the kind of equipment used in the uranium enrichment facility at Natanz, Iran.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=285)** The US admitted in 2012 that it was responsible, together with Israel, for developing Stuxnet.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=292)** The key feature of Stuxnet is that it was designed to be delivered via email or on a USB stick, or through prior implantation on electronic equipment being used in the facility.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=304)** With its design, Stuxnet can get to its target systems even if they're not connected to the internet.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=311)** Stuxnet, when it was first released, used four previously unknown vulnerabilities on Windows computers to propagate and deliver the payload to the SCADA system.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=322)** Once on the system, Stuxnet took advantage of a vulnerability in the Siemens WinCC, PCS 7 SCADA control software, which allowed it to take control of the software and then repeatedly speed up and slow down the centrifuges, causing the aluminum tubes to expand and contract, eventually destroying between 900 and 1000 centrifuges.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=347)** A good source of information on APTs is Kaspersky Labs.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=351)** Here we see the Kaspersky APT site.
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=355)** If we scroll down the screen, we can see the various APTs.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=358)** Let's have a look at Stuxnet.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=360)** By clicking on it and going to the threat, we can see the other APTs which it relate to.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=366)** Shortly after Stuxnet was made public, a similar APT called Duqu was identified, followed by Flame, and a year or so later, Equation.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=378)** Defending against APTs is difficult and it's likely that an APT attack will succeed.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=385)** APTs are usually found when network monitoring detects the installed malware attempting to connect to its command and control systems.
>
> **[6:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=393)** Focusing controls which address each stage of the cyber kill chain provides the opportunity for early detection, and using tools such as Microsoft's arbitrary code guard can help stop them.
>
> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=406)** Nevertheless, APTs will often penetrate their targets and the average time it takes to detect them, once in, is measured in months.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=416)** Advanced persistent threats are very sophisticated forms of malware.
>
> **[7:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-advanced-persistent-threats?u=76281980&t=421)** They're difficult to detect and there's every indication that they're here to stay.

#### Ransomware: A modern form of extortion
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's have a look at a particular form of malware known as ransomware.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=4)** For targets with current backups, being hit with ransomware is just a nuisance but for those without it, it can be a very expensive lesson in practical cybersecurity.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=15)** Instead of stealing information, ransomware encrypts files or storage systems on its target to lock out their legitimate owner and then demands payment for the decryption key.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=28)** Asymmetric encryption is typically used for this.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=32)** I won't delve into the mysteries of asymmetric encryption in this course, but if you'd like to learn more about it, then you can go to the LinkedIn learning page and search for asymmetric encryption.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=42)** There's plenty of courses to choose from to learn more.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=46)** Let's look at a contemporary example of ransomware.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=50)** CryptoLocker emerged in 2013 and was the most prevalent variant of ransomware until mid 2014 when new variants such as TorrentLocker and CryptoWall took over.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=62)** CryptoLocker was extraordinarily successful with an estimated 234,000 victims.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=68)** It used phishing campaigns to opportunistically target its victims.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=73)** These campaigns included the FedEx and UPS you have a parcel emails, which includes a hyperlink to a malicious website where the malware exists.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=83)** Similar ransomware campaigns using bank emails, FBI notices and speeding fines have also been identified.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=91)** The newer version, particularly prevalent in the Netherlands, used a malicious attachment, a Word document with an embedded macro which downloaded the malware directly onto the target computer when the document was opened.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=106)** The person behind the CryptoLocker campaign is a Russian called Evgeny Mikhailovich Bogachev.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=112)** Bogachev used the game over Zeus botnet, a network of commander control servers to distribute CryptoLocker.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=120)** The network was taken down by the FBI in June, 2014 but Bogachev is still at large with a $3 million bounty on his head.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=130)** CryptoLocker executes through a five-stage process.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=134)** Firstly, the victim computer is infected and the malware is installed.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=139)** Then the malware attempts to connect to its command control server.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=144)** It contains an algorithm which randomly creates domain names such as the one shown and tries each of these until it finds one which is active.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=152)** It then generates an encryption key and uses the advanced encryption algorithm, AES, to encrypt files on the target computer.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=162)** At that point, the malware issues a demand for money in order to recover the decryption key.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=168)** CryptoLocker is particularly difficult to recover from as it uses RSA, a strong asymmetric encryption system to encrypt and send the AES decryption key back to the command and control server ensuring it can't be seen.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=184)** After being encrypted, the decryption key can't be recovered directly from the victim's computer.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=191)** More details on CryptoLocker are available from the U.S. cert site shown here.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=197)** When CryptoLocker hits an enterprise, it can have a pretty serious consequence as it not only encrypts files on the employee's computer, but also encrypts files in any shares that the computer has access to.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=210)** For a business which manages its information in a Windows file system, this can be devastating with often tens of thousands of business files rendered inaccessible.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=221)** The criminals running ransomware campaigns usually require payment through financial systems such as Bitcoin or pay safe cards in order to make themselves difficult to trace.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=233)** When ransomware emerged, payment demands were in the order of a few hundred dollars.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=238)** It was often easier for the victim to pay the money than cope with the loss of their files and potentially their ability to run their businesses while they engage in a lengthy and likely futile law enforcement pursuit.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=251)** However, ransomware targeting in demands have changed.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=256)** The Ryuk ransomware targets local government and small to medium businesses, and its ransom demands vary with one victim being asked for 65 Bitcoins, about $600,000 at the time, and they still get paid.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=272)** In 2019, two municipalities in the state of Florida together paid over $1.1 million ransom to recover their data.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=283)** In the 18 months from June, 2021 to November, 2022, the Hive Ransomware Group has netted over a hundred million dollars with its ransomware campaign which focuses on the healthcare sector and it's also extended this campaign to other areas of the critical infrastructure.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ransomware-a-modern-form-of-extortion?u=76281980&t=303)** The attacks use a variety of tactics including exploiting Microsoft Exchange servers and typically gains access through phishing emails.

#### Cryptomining
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cryptomining?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cryptomining?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Lecturer] One of the forms of cyber attack, that we'll come across, is to take over a computer and use it to run what's called mining attacks on a cryptocurrency wallet.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cryptomining?u=76281980&t=11)** This is known as cryptomining or cryptojacking.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cryptomining?u=76281980&t=16)** It's not illegal to carry out cryptomining, quite the opposite.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cryptomining?u=76281980&t=20)** It's a legitimate process that's part of the cryptocurrency system, which requires users to verify transactions by running complex calculations.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cryptomining?u=76281980&t=32)** The result of successfully completing the calculation is that the user gets paid for doing it is cryptocurrency.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cryptomining?u=76281980&t=39)** A new cryptocurrency is generated into the system.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cryptomining?u=76281980&t=44)** However, rather than use their own resources, cryptominers take over our computer and use it to run these calculations.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cryptomining?u=76281980&t=54)** A single computer isn't sufficient to run the calculations, so it becomes just one of many zombie computers in a botnet, which shares out the computation.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cryptomining?u=76281980&t=66)** As a pure cryptomining attack, the malware doesn't damage our files or attempt to steal our credentials, but it does use our system resources without our authorization.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cryptomining?u=76281980&t=78)** However, in a 2017 survey of cryptomining attacks, Trend Micro found that 20% combined cryptomining with other forms of attack, including remote code execution and SQL injections.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cryptomining?u=76281980&t=94)** This makes cryptomining something that we don't want on our systems.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** sql (1)
> **Definitions:** known as (1)
> **Speakers:** - [lecturer] (1)

#### Hardware implants and other cyber FUD
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Speaker] In October, 2018, Bloomberg created a sensation by announcing that the Chinese had implanted tiny chips in the servers of an American company, Super Micro Computer.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=13)** The article reported that the servers were supplied to the Departments of Defense and other sensitive government agencies, as well as Amazon and Apple.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=23)** In a letter sent to its customers, Super Micro reported that their investigations found no evidence of Bloomberg's claim.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=31)** Apple and Amazon also very quickly denied the findings and called on Bloomberg to retract the story.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=37)** Eventually, one of Bloomberg's named sources said that his comments were taken out of context and he actually told Bloomberg that what it had reported didn't make sense.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=48)** While he had discussed the theoretical feasibility with Bloomberg, he'd never suggested that they'd been used in the Super Micro board.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=57)** His overall take on the piece is that the technical details were taken from an earlier black hat presentation he'd made and were jumbled.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=66)** Another hardware FUD was unleashed when the reports started to emerge about two new computer chip exploits called Meltdown and Spectre.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=74)** The initial reports indicated the vulnerabilities in these chips would leak passwords and sensitive data and could be used to steal data from other cloud users.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=84)** Another report from a computer consultancy company suggested that the current standards of security in the tech industry means that it was crucial that businesses contacted their highly qualified cybersecurity team to protect against Meltdown, Specter and future security threats.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=102)** Adding to the drama, CNN reported that a US government backed body warned that the chips themselves needed to be replaced to completely fix the problems.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=112)** One cybersecurity expert announced Meltdown and Specter were disasters and another stated that Meltdown can be exploited by any script kiddie.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=123)** It was suggested that the exploits are nearly impossible to fix short of shipping out new processes.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=129)** As it turned out, firmware patches were shipped quickly and there have been no reports of any successful exploit using the two techniques.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=137)** Meltdown and Spectre were high on drama and low on real risk, and FUD once more trumped common sense.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=147)** Not all reports relating to hardware insecurity are FUD.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=152)** The research has shown here identified flaws in the Trusted Protection module chips from Intel and STMicro, which enabled the extraction of signature keys, breaking the chain of trust for which they form the roots.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=166)** The researchers have been involved in identifying previous hardware vulnerabilities and are respected in their field.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=173)** The vulnerability in this case can be readily exploited.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=178)** For an end user, this particular vulnerability can be exploited via the Target's browser by having it visit a malicious website and it was effective on all versions of Internet Explorer running at the time of the announcement.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=192)** This announcement was one that was worth taking seriously.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hardware-implants-and-other-cyber-fud?u=76281980&t=197)** Nevertheless, when it comes to sensational exposes regarding cybersecurity, check the evidence before you believe it.

### 2. Frameworks for Protection against Cyber Threats

#### The Orange Book: Early concepts in computer security
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Man] The era of trusted computing started in the 1980s with the publication of a series of books on the security requirements for systems used by the United States Department of Defense.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=12)** These books were known as the Rainbow Series of books.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=16)** The best known book in the Rainbow Series is the Orange book, which describe the security design of a computer that can be trusted to handle both unclassified and classified information, known as a multi-level secure, or trusted computer.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=31)** The Orange book describes the security design and subsequent evaluation of security for an information system.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=39)** It introduces four key concepts in information security.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=43)** A reference monitor, which mediates access to system resources, a formal security model for reading and writing information.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=53)** The idea of a trusted computing base as a subsystem containing all the security code and the testing required to achieve various levels of assurance.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=64)** The reference monitor concept is an essential element of any system that provides multi-level secure computing facilities and controls.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=73)** The reference monitor enforces access controls between subjects and objects of the system.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=79)** The subject may be a user or a program module and the object may be a data file or a restricted system function.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=88)** I'll just use the term user as the more common term for subject.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=93)** The reference monitor has three essential characteristics.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=98)** It must be tamper-proof, it must always be invoked, and it must be small enough to be completely analyzed and tested.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=108)** The Orange book introduces the Bell-LaPadula scheme for managing multi-level information flows.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=115)** Using this scheme, the book presents two approaches to security.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=119)** Discretionary access control is used for applying security within the same classification of information to provide a means of restricting information access on a need-to-know basis.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=131)** It requires an access control list to be maintained by an administrator who authorizes subjects to access objects.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=138)** This is the normal folder and file control scheme we use today.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=144)** Mandatory access control is the Bell-LaPadula scheme in which each subject holds a certain level of access rights or clearance and an object is labeled at a certain level of sensitivity.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=156)** The security labels which define the level of sensitivity in the Orange book include restricted, confidential-secret and top-secret.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=166)** Mandatory access control has two rules.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=169)** The first rule is the simple security rule, which states that a user at a certain clearance level cannot read anything which has a label at a higher sensitivity level, which by definition they do not have access to.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=182)** The second rule is the star security rule, which states that a user at a certain clearance level cannot write down into a file which is labeled at a lower level, as this may expose sensitive information to subjects not clear to access it.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=199)** The heart of a trusted computer system is its trusted computing base, which contains the elements of the system responsible for security, all within the security perimeter.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=210)** The TCB includes hardware, firmware, and software critical to protection.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=215)** It must be designed and implemented such that nothing outside the trusted computing base is sensitive or relevant to managing security.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=224)** A TCB should be as simple as possible, consistent with the functions it has to perform, in order to enable adequate testing.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=232)** The final and probably most important part of the Orange book is the classification scheme is introduced for evaluating assurance of systems.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=241)** In short, the scheme provides for four levels of system assurance, within each level, there are one or more tiers.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=249)** The levels are D1, C1 to C2, B1 to B3, and A1.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/the-orange-book-early-concepts-in-computer-security?u=76281980&t=259)** We'll look further at security assurance levels later in the course.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** known as (2), is an  (1)
> **Code Keywords:** module (1), function (1)
> **Env Vars:** tcb (2)
> **Cross-References:** later in (1)
> **Prerequisites:** required to (1)
> **Speakers:** - [man] (1)

#### Understanding the NIST Cybersecurity Framework
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The inclusion of cyberspace, International critical infrastructures was formally recognized at the third Global Conference on cyberspace, held in Seoul in 2013.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=12)** With the publication of the Seoul Framework for a Commitment to an Open and Secure Cyberspace.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=19)** It states, "The global and open nature of the internet is a driving force in accelerating progress towards development.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=27)** Governments, business organizations, and individual owners and users of cyberspace must assume responsibility for and take steps to enhance the security of their information technologies."
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=41)** In response to this, in 2014, the US National Institute of Standards and Technology issued the framework for improving critical infrastructure cybersecurity.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=53)** In 2024, the NIST CSF version 2 was issued.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=58)** This framework has now become the defacto standard for cybersecurity.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=63)** Let's take a look at it.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=66)** The NIST Cybersecurity Framework is an action oriented approach to security and consists of three elements.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=73)** The framework core, the framework profile, and the framework implementation tiers.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=79)** The framework core provides a set of activities to achieve cybersecurity, described in the six areas of govern, identify, protect, detect, respond, and recover.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=93)** Each of these activities is decomposed into a total of 22 categories of security activities.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=100)** For example, we can see that the detect group decomposes into the two categories of continuous monitoring and adverse event analysis.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=112)** Going deeper, we can see that each of the categories is further decomposed into a set of controls.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=117)** For example, the technology infrastructure resilience category is broken down into four subcategories, network and environment protection, environmental protection, resilience, and capacity management.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=133)** Each of these subcategories is referenced to the relevant NIST, ISO and COBIT standards.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=141)** The NIST Cybersecurity framework doesn't introduce its own set of controls.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=146)** It provides a higher level framework, which can be used to develop a contemporary cybersecurity profile for an organization, but it relies on existing control frameworks for its implementation.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=160)** COBIT, ISA, or otherwise known as IEC 62443.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework-24575120?u=76281980&t=166)** ISO 27000 and NIST, SP 800-53

#### Adopting the NIST Cybersecurity Framework
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] The second component of the NIST Cybersecurity Framework is the framework profile.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=6)** This is used to align business outcomes and cybersecurity activities, providing a view of risks and a development plan to bridge the two.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=16)** The third and final component of the NIST Cybersecurity Framework is a maturity model for cybersecurity known as the implementation tiers.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=26)** The basic level of cybersecurity maturity is the partial implementation tier.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=32)** This is characterized by enterprise risk management being somewhat ad hoc and reactive, where cybersecurity activities aren't based on risk objectives or business outcomes and where there's little external collaboration.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=47)** At the next level of maturity, risk management practices are formalized but may not be adopted across the enterprise.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=55)** There's informal sharing of cybersecurity information internally, but not externally.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=61)** The third tier of maturity, repeatable, is where risk management is formalized and mandated as policy and processes exist to respond to changes in risk.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=72)** Collaboration and information sharing exist both internally and externally.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=79)** The highest maturity level, adaptive, extends the third level with the awareness and agility to apply continuous changes to cybersecurity activities as a result of changes to assets, threat, and vulnerabilities.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=94)** When adopting the cybersecurity framework for an organization, NIST recommends establishing two profiles.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=101)** The first should represent the current state of cybersecurity as assessed against the subset of enterprise-specific activities that have been selected as being required.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=113)** This is what cybersecurity looks like now.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=116)** The second should be the target state of cybersecurity, set as the acceptable level of risk against each of the enterprise-specific activities.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=125)** A security plan of prioritized projects can then be defined to close the gap between the current and the target state framework profiles.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=136)** For an organization that's starting up its cybersecurity program, there are some key actions required to take advantage of the cybersecurity framework.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=145)** The first is to identify the key business outcomes and then understand the threats and vulnerabilities to those outcomes.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/adopting-the-nist-cybersecurity-framework?u=76281980&t=154)** Create a profile, conduct a risk assessment, decide on the target profile, determine, analyze, and prioritize the gaps to create the action plan, and establish and execute a program to implement the plan.

> **Definitions:** is a  (1), known as (1)
> **Code Keywords:** extends (1)
> **Prerequisites:** required to (1)
> **Speakers:** - [narrator] (1)

#### Understanding the basics of cyber risk
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Risk is an essential part of doing business, whether it's taking a risk on a merger or acquisition, taking a risk that purchasing new equipment will be a cost-effective investment, or whether there's sufficient plant protection to avoid injuries to workers.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=16)** Management needs to understand their level of risk exposure and make sure that it's within their risk appetite.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=24)** Cybersecurity is at its heart, the management of risk related to internet connected businesses.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=30)** This includes the threat of hackers and malicious software entering from the internet, the vulnerabilities of internet facing-IT systems and the attack countermeasures or controls, all of which affect how successful the business will be in meeting desired outcomes.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=49)** A standard approach to managing risk has been developed by the National Institute of Standards and Technology and its application is described in Special Publication 830, Guide for Conducting Risk Assessments.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=63)** The International Standards Organization also provides guidance with its ISO 27005, Information Security Risk Management Publication.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=74)** While there are minor terminology differences the intent of both documents is the same.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=81)** Cyber risk focuses on the information technology assets we operate and the services we deliver.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=88)** It starts with a threat event which must be analyzed to determine that the event will occur.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=95)** This is done by considering the likely threat actors, their capabilities, and their resources.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=101)** The intent or motivation needs to be considered.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=104)** What is it that drives this threat actor to want to mount an attack?
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=109)** An attacker will only attack if the results outweigh the cost of attacking and that depends on the value of the target to the threat actor.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=117)** This could be financial gain, obtaining intelligence, disrupting services or just peer recognition.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=124)** Think about the scam emails you've received.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=127)** Who is the likely threat actor?
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=129)** Might it be just a student looking for a couple of extra dollars or could it be an East European organized crime gang?
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=137)** Think about their capabilities and what it means for the sophistication of the attack.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=142)** The next risk issue to consider is the set of vulnerabilities in the information and processing assets, databases, workstations, servers and networks, which this event can exploit to cause damage.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=160)** For instance, a data center in the basement of a building may be vulnerable to a flood event.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=166)** A website may have a software vulnerability which can be exploited.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=171)** A server without an uninterruptable power supply would be vulnerable to a power outage.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=177)** However, not all vulnerabilities are equal.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=181)** A flood may be catastrophic with the whole data center out of action for weeks, whereas an attack which exploits a website floor may just be a nuisance.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=192)** Then we consider the impact to the business in the event the threat is realized.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=196)** This is typically done by carrying out a business impact assessment to determine what systems will be affected, how this flows on to business processes and the cost of service degradation or failure to meet critical levels of performance.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=212)** Being able to describe a security event as a business impact is a powerful way of gaining the attention and the respect of the business and being able to get a well-balanced business decision on what to do about the threat.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=226)** Controls may have been put in place to protect the asset and their effectiveness needs to be considered for each of the feasible threat scenarios.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-basics-of-cyber-risk?u=76281980&t=236)** This will then allow calculation of the overall risk.

#### Analyzing cyber threats and controls

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The cybersecurity risk management program starts with sourcing threat intelligence.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=6)** Let's have a look at some sources of threat intelligence.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=10)** A useful catalog of threats can be found at Appendix E to the NIST Special Publication.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=16)** This catalog provides representative examples of adversarial threat events expressed as tactics, techniques, and procedures, or TTPs, and non-adversarial threat events.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=30)** Another useful source of TTPs is the Mitre ATT&CK site, which is used in Mitre's Cybersecurity Resiliency Framework.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=37)** This is a detailed source of information on who the threat actors are and how they carry out their cyber attacks.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=45)** Many of the threats that have been turned into exploits and are being seen in cyber attacks are listed in the Exploit-DB database.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=54)** For example, here we see the details of an exploit against the SmartRG Router.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=62)** There are a number of companies that publish malware analysis reports, such as this one produced by VMRay.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=69)** These are useful for gaining an insight into the contemporary techniques being used by attackers.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=75)** There are four possible treatments once an assessment has identified the risk: risk acceptance, where the risk is within the business's appetite, risk avoidance, where it's better to stop doing that line of business than take the risk, risk transfer, where a third party takes the risk, such as insurance to cover the risk should it eventuate, and risk mitigation, where controls are implemented to reduce risk.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=104)** Risk mitigation, and the protection of business outcomes which they provide means implementing controls in the form of cybersecurity policies, processes, and technical solutions.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/analyzing-cyber-threats-and-controls?u=76281980&t=117)** We'll cover controls shortly.

#### Recording, reporting, and the risk context
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A key part of risk management is maintaining a record of the risks that have been identified, and where relevant, tracking the progress of work to reduce the risk.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=10)** The normal way to record risks is in a risk register.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=14)** This could be a manual record, but more usually it's automated.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=19)** The basic form of automation is using a spreadsheet as shown here.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=24)** Larger organizations may use the more sophisticated governance risk and controls, or GRC solutions, although the principle as far as managing risk is the same.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=36)** The risk register contains the basic risk information, such as an ID and name, classification information, and the risk owner.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=43)** It also contains a summary of the consequences of a risk being realized.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=49)** The risk information is usually presented in two ways.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=52)** The first is the inherent risk, assuming no controls are in place.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=57)** This is useful to know because it determines how strong the controls need to be.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=63)** The higher the risk, the stronger the control.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=67)** Then the control details are provided and a residual risk is calculated to show the current risk that is being experienced by the business.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=76)** Take a few minutes to set up your own spreadsheet risk register as we've just discussed, and add an entry, malware infection.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=85)** Think about your own situation.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=87)** What could be the root cause, the consequence, and the inherent risk level?
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=93)** What controls do you have in place, and what is the residual level of risk?
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=98)** (jazzy music) Risks can be shown as bubbles on what's known as a risk heat map, where individual risks are charted in the cell which exists at the intersection of the likelihood row and the impact column.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=115)** This is sometimes called a risk bubble chart.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=118)** A typical approach to managing risks is to accept any very low risks which appear in the green area.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=126)** Low risks shown in the gold area are accepted but monitored to ensure they don't increase.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=133)** Medium risks in the yellow area are scheduled for routine remediation work, and high and very high risks are shown together here in the red area and require immediate remediation.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=147)** This form of risk chart is very common and it provides a succinct way to present a high-level picture of the risks.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=154)** Sometimes the bubble chart is enhanced to show the plan progress of mitigations using an arrow and a bubble to identify the final expected risk level after mitigation.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=165)** This is a powerful way to show the work being done to reduce risk.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=170)** The term "risk context" refers to the risk bubble chart in the tables used to determine likelihood and impact.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=177)** Here we can see the tables representing the five levels of likelihood, which make up the vertical axis on the heat map and the multiple tables representing different perspectives on impact, which together make up the horizontal axis.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=193)** These tables are typically developed specifically for the business by their risk officer.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/recording-reporting-and-the-risk-context?u=76281980&t=199)** The risk context should also include guidance on the actions required to be taken at each risk level, reflecting more urgent action and more intense oversight at the higher levels of business risk.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** known as (1), is a  (1), refers to (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Analogies:** such as (1), picture (1)
> **Prerequisites:** set up (1), required to (1)
> **Code Keywords:** require (1)
> **Env Vars:** grc (1)
