#### An advanced risk framework
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] NIST, in December 2018, issued revision 2 to their original special publication on risk: SP 800-37: Risk Management Framework.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=12)** The Risk Management Framework provides a disciplined, structured, and flexible process for managing security and privacy risk.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=20)** It covers information security categorization, control selection, implementation, and assessment, system and common control authorizations, and continuous monitoring of risks.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=34)** The Risk Management Framework considers risk at three levels: information systems risk, mission or business process risk, and whole of business risk.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=46)** The risk management process involves preparation of the necessary risk material needed to carry out risk management.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=53)** And then a six-stage process of categorization, selection, implementation, assessment, authorization, and monitoring.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=63)** The prepare stage of the framework involves seven actions, three of which are risk-related and four controls-related.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=72)** They are: assign people to risk management roles; prepare the risk management context, also known as the risk strategy; complete an organization-wide risk assessment; establish control baselines according to the standards relevant to the organization; identify common controls and prioritize them according to the potential impact of an attack; and develop the plan for monitoring control effectiveness.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=103)** The categorize phase overlaps somewhat with the prepare phase, as it requires a full review of the IT systems in use, particularly identifying the system characteristics and the information they process and store.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=117)** The next step is to determine the impact levels to confidentiality, availability, and integrity.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=124)** And the final step is to get business endorsement or authorization of the three impact classification levels.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=133)** The select stage requires that controls are selected and tailored to the specific system environment, to mitigate all risks to the system that are beyond risk appetite.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=144)** This is judged by determining the risk level, and then now identifying from the risk context whether controls are required.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=152)** The steps in the select stage are: control selection, either by adopting a baseline set of controls, by a custom set of controls driven by the risk assessments, or by a combination of both; control tailoring to suit the operating environment; control allocation to systems, ensuring that the specific business requirements for security in that system are met across people, process, and technology; documenting the controls for each system in a system security plan; developing and implementing the approach to continuous monitoring of control effectiveness; and gain business approval of the system security plans and continuous monitoring process.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=198)** The next stage is to implement the controls that have been identified for the system, and maintain the system security plans accordingly.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=207)** The assess stage is about through-life assessment of the system to ensure that controls are effective, and there is no evidence of a breach.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=216)** There are seven steps in this stage of the risk management life cycle: assess a selection based on candidate qualifications and target knowledge; develop the plan for the assessment; and carry out the assessment plan for the controls; report on the control effectiveness, providing findings and recommendations; remediate any findings that can be immediately rectified; and develop an overall plan of action for findings that can't be immediately rectified.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=250)** The purpose of the authorize stage is to provide organizational accountability, by requiring a senior manager to determine if the security and privacy risk represented by the overall set of risk management activities and plans is acceptable.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=267)** This stage has five steps: for developing the submission, management review additional risk management response to any issues raised; approval of decisions for each system; and an authorization report.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=283)** The final stage is monitoring.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=285)** This is a key stage of the framework which provides the ongoing situational awareness about the security and privacy posture of the information system and the organization in support of risk management decisions, and includes some of the above stages.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/an-advanced-risk-framework?u=76281980&t=301)** It has seven steps: the systems and environment are monitored for any changes that might occur, in-flight assessments are performed as required, any issues identified are responded to, risk management documents are maintained, and security and privacy risks are reported regularly, authorizations are given to systems as required, and systems are securely disposed of when no longer required.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** known as (1), is a  (1)
> **Env Vars:** nist (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Managing security with COBIT
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the more important IT frameworks for the enterprise is COBIT, the Control Objectives for IT.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=8)** COBIT is published by the Information Systems' Audit and Control Association, ISACA, and its purpose is to ensure that enterprises have in place an effective and auditable set of governance and management processes for IT, which deliver value for its stakeholders.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=27)** COBIT is designed around a set of processes.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=30)** These are grouped into the four areas of plan, build, deliver, and monitor.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=36)** We can see at the top left the Plan group, known in full as a Align, Plan and Organize, with its 14 APO processes.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=45)** Below that is the Build, Acquire, and Implement group.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=49)** It has 11 processes.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=52)** At the bottom of the diagram are the six processes in the Deliver, Service and Support group.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=59)** And to the right is the Monitor, Evaluate and Assess group with its four processes.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=66)** The COBIT framework is used by the financial sector for carrying out IT general controls external audits.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=74)** Consequently, having a COBIT aligned security framework is the first step in putting in place an IT environment which will meet regulatory obligations.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=86)** From a cybersecurity perspective, there are two key processes for security.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=92)** APO13, Managed Security, in the Plan group, and DSS05, Managed Security Services in the Deliver group.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=105)** Of course, there are many other processes in which security plays a part.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=109)** For example, security incident management is an important activity but this falls within the overall IT process of DSS02, Managed Service Requests and Incidents.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=123)** Let's take a look into APO13 Managed Security which defines the requirement for security management.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=131)** The process description is define, operate and monitor a system for information security management.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=138)** And it has five goals: support IT and business compliance, support the management of IT and enterprise risk.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=148)** Contribute to the transparency of IT costs and benefits.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=153)** Ensure the security of information, infrastructure, and applications and provide reliable information for decision making.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=162)** APO13 consists of three control objectives.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=166)** APO13.01, establish and maintain an Enterprise Information Security Management System.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=175)** APO13.02, define and manage a security plan which establishes a set of objectives to progress towards the desired security posture and APO13.03, monitor and review the ISMS.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=192)** The Enterprise Information Security Management System, or ISMS, defines the approach taken to ensuring information security is effective.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=201)** And this is often aligned to the set of requirements outlined in the international standard ISO 27001, information security management systems requirements.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/managing-security-with-cobit?u=76281980&t=213)** While IPO 1301 is a single control objective, to satisfy it involves putting in place a number of lower level controls from the ISO 27000 series of standards.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Analogies:** for example (1)

#### COBIT for operational security
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's look now at the second security-focused process, DSS05-Managed Security Services.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=8)** The description of this process is to protect enterprise information to maintain the level of information security risk acceptable to the enterprise in accordance with the security policy, establish and maintain information security roles and access privileges, and perform security monitoring.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=30)** Essentially, DSS05 defines the requirements of operational security.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=37)** DSS05 provides operational processes to satisfy three of the five APO13 goals, support IT and business compliance, support the management of IT and enterprise risk, and ensure the security of information, infrastructure, and applications.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=58)** There are seven operational security control objectives in DSS05, which provide the foundation for a defensive cybersecurity program.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=67)** Let's take a look at what's in each of them.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=70)** The first control objective is protect against malware.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=74)** Malware is one of the main challenges in cybersecurity today, and protecting against it involves a number of activities and controls.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=82)** These include antivirus, security patching, security information awareness, and in contemporary terms, cyber threat intelligence, change management, security filtering of email and web traffic, and security training.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=100)** The second control objective is manage network and connectivity security.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=107)** This includes establishing and enforcing policy on network connections, enforcement of password entry, the configuration and use of firewalls and intrusion detection systems, network security protocols and communications encryption, network configuration, security mechanisms to ensure trusted transmission and receipt, network security control testing and penetration testing.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=138)** A critical control for this objective is network segregation.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=144)** Manage endpoint security covers the security of laptops, desktops, servers, mobile devices, and network equipment.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=153)** It requires that controls are put in place to ensure the endpoints are securely configured, hardened to remove unnecessary ports and protocols, and that remote access is managed.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=167)** The next control objective is manage user identity and logical access.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=172)** Identity and access management is a very complex issue and this one control objective can easily consume half the effort in a mature cybersecurity program.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=183)** It's also the area which generates a good proportion of all audit findings, so it pays to keep a tight focus on it.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=193)** This process requires that identities are managed from creation to removal, access rights are established and maintained in line with the roles and responsibilities of the organization, that access to systems and information is authorized and authenticated, that privileged access is strictly controlled, and that access rights are regularly reviewed, and that appropriate audit trails of access are kept.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=223)** The fifth of these control objectives covers the management of physical access to IT assets.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=228)** This includes perimeter protection, such as fences, doors, and locks, intruder detection systems, access controls for data centers and office spaces, identity cards and visitor-management procedures.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=245)** Increasingly, the use of cloud-based infrastructure is reducing the effort required to manage this area but increasing the dependence on and the oversight of third-party security.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=257)** Managing sensitive documents is an increasingly important aspect of security, as the focus of protective measures shrinks from the perimeter to the information itself.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=269)** With employees taking laptops out of the enterprise and sending data out to mobile devices, perimeter security devices such as corporate firewalls no longer protect enterprise information.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=282)** New techniques such as digital rights management and mobile email encryption need to be employed.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=289)** This process also includes information and device-centric controls, such as passwords or pin-controlled printing and pin codes on mobile devices.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=301)** Finally, monitor the infrastructure for security-related events provides the detective controls which are needed to identify security breaches should the enterprise's preventative controls fail.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=313)** This control objective includes the operation of intrusion detection and prevention systems, logging and alerting security-related events, operating log management in security information and event-monitoring systems, delivering security incidents to the incident-management process, carrying out forensics, and managing evidence.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cobit-for-operational-security?u=76281980&t=338)** These are all key activities for a cybersecurity operations center.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** dss05 (4), apo13 (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (4)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), finally, (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1), is an  (1)

#### Introduction to cybersecurity controls
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The term cybersecurity means to protect things in cyberspace from attack.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=5)** And we do this by using security controls.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=9)** When designing our controls we need to make sure they're fit for purpose.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=13)** Firstly, we need to check whether the cost of the control is more or less than the loss associated with the impact of the attack.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=22)** We often see a curve graph to explain this where we plot the cost of an increasingly powerful control against the benefit it provides.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=31)** Where the benefit in additional risk reduction outweighs the cost of achieving it, we don't proceed with any further control.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=39)** Secondly, we need to consider how effective the control is against the threat.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=44)** The result of assessing the risk based on the likelihood an impact of a threat is known as the inherent risk.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=52)** When controls are implemented, they'll usually be an acceptably small level of risk remaining which is known as the residual risk.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=61)** We can apply what's known as a multi-tiered or defense in depth control strategy to mitigate cyber risks.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=69)** There are four key types of controls that can be applied and it's generally recommended that two or more are used together.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=76)** The first is deterrent controls.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=78)** These reduce the threat.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=80)** An example of this is incarceration which deters would be criminals from carrying out their attacks.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=88)** Preventative controls are designed to stop the attack from succeeding by not allowing it to get at an asset to exploit a vulnerability.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=97)** A firewall is one such preventative control, blocking protocols that might be used as attack vectors.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=104)** Detective controls are used to detect that an attack has taken place.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=109)** A burglar alarm is a typical detective control.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=113)** And finally, corrective controls are used to reduce the impact of an incident.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=118)** And a good example of this is recovery from data backups.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=124)** The NIST Cybersecurity Framework presents corrective as the respond and recover functional controls.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=131)** Let's look at an example.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=133)** In order to protect data from an authorized modification, we firstly apply access controls to ensure that anyone trying to access the data has been authorized.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=144)** In case this fails, we then monitor for any data changes.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/introduction-to-cybersecurity-controls?u=76281980&t=149)** If data is changed maliciously, we can recover it by restoring from backup.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** known as (3), is a  (1)
> **Code Keywords:** finally, (1), let (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Env Vars:** nist (1)
> **Best Practices:** recommended (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Cybersecurity control framework
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] While controls can be applied by an enterprise as a customized response to business risks, in many cases, an external authority will direct that a predefined set of controls be adopted as a baseline for security.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=15)** An example of government policy is SP 800-53, Security and Privacy Controls for Federal Information Systems and Organizations, which federal organizations are required to adopt.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=29)** An authority may be an industry body, such as the Payment Card Industry Council, which requires that merchants adhere to the Payment Card Industry Data Security Standard.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=42)** NIST's SP 800-53 is one of two important control frameworks used in cybersecurity, the other being ISO 27002.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=52)** They're both structured as a set of control categories within each existing number of specific controls.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=60)** While the categories and the controls are different for each standard, they can be mapped against each other.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=67)** These two control frameworks are widely referenced by other security schemes.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=71)** In particular, the NIST Cybersecurity Framework.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=75)** The controls in ISO 27002 are described in a three-tier hierarchy of security category, security control objective, and control.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=86)** Let's have a look at an example.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=88)** Here, we can see access control is the main category, operating system access control is the control objective, and user identification and authentication is the control.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=102)** The NIST SP 800-53 controls are described in a two-tier hierarchy.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=108)** In this example, identity and authentication is the control family and identity and authentication, organizational users, is the control.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=119)** The description is very similar to the description of the ISO 11.5.2 control.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=126)** An important first stage in implementing a control framework is to create what's known as a Statement of Applicability.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=133)** The Statement of Applicability is the main link between the risk assessment and the selection of controls, and its purpose is to provide evidence that all controls have been considered.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=145)** The controls that aren't applicable won't be implemented, and the rationale for emitting them is recorded in the Statement of Applicability.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=154)** Developing a clear Statement of Applicability is a good way to reduce the effort required to meet and maintain a compliant and effective security posture.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=165)** There are a number of specific considerations around controls.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=170)** Common controls can be inherited by one or more systems, reducing both deployment and ongoing operational effort and cost.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=178)** Where specific controls are called for but are either not yet present or can't be implemented, then compensating controls will be required, such as sample checks of manual authorizations in the absence of an electronic authorization process.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=196)** Once a control has been implemented, it needs regular testing, and this should be a routine part of any compliance program.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=204)** Control testing involves two stages: testing design effectiveness, and testing operational effectiveness.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=212)** Design effectiveness is checked by verifying that the control, as implemented, meets the original design requirements.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=220)** For example, to carry out a design test of control ISO 11.5.2, user identification and authentication would involve verifying configuration files.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=232)** To confirm, the taxes to the system requires entry of a user identifier and that a password or some other form of authentication is required prior to allowing access into the system.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=245)** Operational effectiveness involves testing the system and making sure that the control is continuing to be effective against attack.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-control-framework?u=76281980&t=253)** For example, a penetration testing might attempt an SQL injection on the user identifier field in a log-on form to see whether access can be gained without entering valid credentials.

#### Cybersecurity standards of good practice
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are a number of industry standards of good practice which provide guidance on cybersecurity.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=6)** The most well-known is the ISF Standard of Good Practice.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=10)** It's essentially a risk and control framework for managing cybersecurity.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=16)** The Standard of Good Practice is consistent with the major recognized information security standards such as ISO 27002, the NIST Cybersecurity Framework, COVIT and PCI DSS control standards.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=30)** It also aligns with the controls required to satisfy Europe's General Data Privacy Regulations.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=37)** It incorporates the ISF Risk Assessment Methodology or IRAM, which presents a risk management scheme with the three phases of business impact assessment, threat and vulnerability assessment and control selection.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=53)** Let's have a look at what the ISF controls look like.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=56)** The ISF standard of good practice structures its controls into categories, areas, and topics.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=63)** Let's have a look at the security monitoring and improvement category.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=67)** It has two areas and eight topics.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=70)** The two areas are security audits, with its five topics and security improvement, with its three.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=77)** If we dig down into security monitoring, topic S 12.1, it has a principle.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=85)** The information security condition of the organization should be monitored regularly and reported to executive management.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=93)** And an objective.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=94)** To provide the executive management with an accurate, comprehensive, and coherent assessment of the information security condition of the organization.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=104)** The standard of good practice is a comprehensive industry approach to security but only available to members of the Information Security Forum.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=113)** The Central Bank of the Netherlands, DNB, has published a cybersecurity standard of good practice as guidance for the financial sector.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=122)** This is freely available from their website.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=125)** As we can see it takes a risk and testing perspective on controls.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=132)** The standard is structured into categories with each having one or more controls.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=137)** There are almost 60 controls detailed in the standard across these categories.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=142)** The standard contains a maturity model in support of the process category.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=147)** Here we see five levels of maturity, starting with initial and progressing through repeatable, defined, managed and measured to continuous improvement.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=159)** Each level builds on the previous ones and adds more rigor to the process at each step.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=166)** Here we see one of the controls.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=169)** This is the DNB standard of good practice guidance on security and monitoring.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/cybersecurity-standards-of-good-practice?u=76281980&t=175)** The DNB standard isn't as well known as the ISF standard but it is free and it contains a lot of valuable guidance.

### 3. Making Cybersecurity Effective

#### Architecting for security
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Security doesn't exist in isolation.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=3)** It's a characteristic of a business service, a business system and business information.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=9)** All of these are either secure or not secure.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=12)** Although in reality, security isn't black and white, it most definitely comes in shades of gray.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=18)** Nevertheless, organizations will often adopt a set of controls to secure their IT systems and not consider whether or not these reflect any of the requirements of the business.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=30)** Rather than just adopt a generic control set, we can use what's known as enterprise security architecture to architect a security solution which meets the needs of our business and then apply the controls that are necessary to achieve that architecture.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=48)** One of the most popular enterprise security architecture frameworks is SABSA, the Sherwood Applied Business Security Architecture.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=56)** SABSA is used to capture business requirements and then determine what security is needed to meet those requirements.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=65)** The basic construct in SABSA is its architecture matrix.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=70)** The two top layers, the contextual and conceptual, contain the elements of the architecture necessary in the strategy and planning stage.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=79)** While the three lower layers, the logical physical and component, contain those necessary to design secure IT systems and processes to support the higher level business goals and objectives.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=93)** This matrix is used to capture all relevant security concepts and activities for the enterprise and these are shown in summary in the cells.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=103)** Security is often defined in terms of the information assurance we achieve by considering a system's confidentiality, integrity and availability.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=115)** This approach came from early work on models of security and while it's a very common approach, it's also a very constricting and artificial paradigm.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=124)** Confidentiality, integrity and availability are indeed three attributes but they're not the only ones.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=133)** We can add more such as non-repudiation, authenticity, utility.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=140)** But to create an effective information security architecture that's business centric rather than security centric, this is still inadequate.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=149)** The SABSA framework provides a comprehensive set of business security attributes, which have been collected from hundreds of consultancy projects, and most, if not all, attributes that an organization needs to define their own concept of security can be found here.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=167)** SABSA's many attributes are grouped into seven categories in what's called an attribute taxonomy: user, management, operational, risk, technical, legal, and business.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=178)** These categories represent the focus of the business outcome which is being protected by the attributes.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=184)** This is in effect a picture of what business success looks like.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=190)** This set of attributes are often used as a pick list from which to choose a relevant subset of attributes for an architecture project and they're quite useful as a cross check on the attributes derived from the business.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=204)** We can show the attributes in a business relevant form.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=208)** Here, we show the attributes representing activities which are important to the military, preparation of the force, intelligence regarding the battlefield, the characteristics of operations, commanding and sustaining the force and providing protection of the force.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=226)** When we architect security, we start with business goals and objectives.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=232)** Here's a strategic business construct showing goals and objectives.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=237)** The smaller ellipses together represent the objectives which are required to meet the goals in the larger ellipses.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=243)** We can analyze the objectives to determine what we need in terms of security attributes to ensure the objectives are met.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=252)** In this table, we can see some general business objectives, we call these business drivers, which map to a number of the individual enterprise technology and business division objectives.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=265)** For each of these, we can describe the business driver as a set of security focused attributes.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=272)** We can then use these attributes to measure security across the organization and we can map them down to the information systems which support the various business processes.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=284)** By measuring the effect of a security incident on an attribute, we can map this easily back to the business goals and objectives, which depend upon it.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/architecting-for-security?u=76281980&t=294)** There's much more to SABSA and enterprise security architecture in general but the main takeaway is that we need to always look at security through business eyes because security is what we need to achieve business success.

#### Protecting payment card data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Cyber criminals understand that credit cards are a lucrative target for attack.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=6)** The payment card industry governing body, the PCI Council, has responded to this threat by issuing the PCI Data Security Standard as an actionable framework for developing a robust security regime for cardholder data.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=25)** More recently, and in the light of state sponsored attacks on personal and government data identified in the Snowden leaks, government regulators have enacted regulatory requirements for notification of data breaches.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=39)** In particular, the European General Data Protection Regulations.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=45)** This has increased the business liability in the event of a data breach.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=49)** It's now critically important for any business taking payments through credit cards to protect their information and transactions.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=58)** It helps to understand the terminology of PCI when reviewing the Data Security Standard.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=65)** Let's look at the key terms.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=67)** A merchant is someone who takes a credit card or debit card as a form of payment.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=74)** A service provider is someone who provides a service that is used for payment card information storage, or transactions of a merchant.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=83)** A qualified security assessor is an independent person certified to report on PCI compliance.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=92)** An internal security assessor works for the merchant, and is certified to submit a self-assessment.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=100)** A data breach is a failure of security, which results in the loss of cardholder information.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=107)** Cardholder data, or information, is the primary account number, the cardholder name, the expiration date, and the service code.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=117)** The sensitive authentication data is the encoded data on the magnetic stripe or chip, the card verification value, and the pin.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=128)** In the event of a data breach, the card company will launch an investigation to determine the cause.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=134)** If the company has its PCI compliance, it can claim safe harbor.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=139)** If not, it could face a hefty fine, or removal of its right to accept credit cards.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=146)** Regardless, it will face remediation costs, which could include card replacement and possibly customer compensation.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=155)** With that background to PCI, let's now look at the PCI Data Security Standard itself.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=161)** The standard provides a set of actionable controls together with testing procedures to provide a clear definition of what has to be done to achieve compliance.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=172)** Version 3.2 of the standard provides 12 technical and operational requirements areas, covering almost 200 mandatory controls.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=181)** Let's have a look at some of the key controls for the first six requirements areas.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=187)** The first requirement is to have an effective firewall configuration.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=192)** This means that firewall configuration standards have been set, that all firewall changes are tested, and that the rule sets are reviewed every six months.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=203)** A network diagram must be maintained for any part of the network that stores, transmits, or interfaces to payment card data.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=212)** And data flows across the network need to be defined.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=216)** Traffic not related to cardholder transactions must be denied access to the cardholder systems.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=223)** So it's normal to have a segregated PCI zone on the internal network so that the traffic can be managed at the PCI zone gateway.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=232)** A demilitarized zone is required for any systems with direct internet access, and this needs to be firewalled at both the internal and external gateways.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=242)** Firewalls are not just for the enterprise.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=245)** Mobile devices, including any employee-owned devices that are allowed to be connected to the internal network, must have personal firewall software installed and operational, with a configuration that's not able to be changed by the employee.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=260)** This is a key consideration when thinking about bring your own device, or BYOD, environments.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=268)** The next requirement is that all default passwords and insecure configuration settings are changed.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=275)** Security configuration standards are required for each device and system component to allow effective hardening, and all necessary reports and services should be removed.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=287)** Stored cardholder data has to be kept to a minimum and protected, and no sensitive authentication data can be stored, even in encrypted form, once authentication has been completed.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=300)** The account number, when displayed, must be masked, typically by replacing all except the last four digits with asterisks.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=308)** When stored, the account number must be protected through strong cryptography, or one-way hashing.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=315)** Key management is a critical part of any cryptographic solution, and must be implemented effectively.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=323)** Transmitted cardholder data on open networks, such as the internet and unprotected wireless networks, must be encrypted.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=331)** And then user systems, such as email and messaging, must never be used to send unprotected account numbers.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=338)** The cryptographic scheme must be effective, and what's effective may change over time.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=345)** For many years, the Secure Sockets Layer, SSL, had been a common cryptographic solution for web access.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=353)** In 2013, a fundamental vulnerability in the scheme was detected and exploited in the Heartbleed vulnerability.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=362)** Subsequently, the PCI Security Standards Council determined that the Secure Sockets Layer protocol was no longer an acceptable solution for the protection of cardholder data.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=374)** Systems processing cardholder data must implement antivirus software to provide protection against malware on both endpoint devices and servers.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=385)** As some malware may enter a system prior to its signature being included in the antivirus database, regular scans must also be undertaken.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=395)** Threats and vulnerabilities should be monitored through vendor alerts and threat intelligence feeds, and critical security patches must be installed within one month of release.
>
> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=406)** Development and test accounts must be removed before systems are put into production, and custom development must include source code review prior to implementation, with special attention given to common vulnerabilities, such as SQL injection and cross-site scripting.
>
> **[7:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=424)** Production account numbers must not be used for testing.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=428)** The next six security requirements in the PCI Data Security Standard address the level of security required outside of the PCI environment.
>
> **[7:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=438)** Of particular interest is the requirement to restrict physical access, which extends to special purpose devices used to read cards.
>
> **[7:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=448)** ATMs, and more recently embedded readers in devices such as gas pumps, are regularly targeted by criminals who install skimmers, which can copy credit card data.
>
> **[7:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=460)** These are big business.
>
> **[7:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=461)** In April, 2015, a sweep of 6,000 gas stations in Florida found 81 skimmers attached to gas pumps.
>
> **[7:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=471)** This particular scam has been estimated to make, in the US, as much as $3 billion a year for criminals.
>
> **[7:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=479)** Let's revisit the Information is Beautiful data breaches site, shown here.
>
> **[8:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=485)** At the far left of the graphic, there's the May, 2021 data breach into Air India, where credit card details were leaked.
>
> **[8:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=494)** Check the number of customers for which details were taken, and assuming all had credit card details stored, calculate what this breach was worth to the criminals if each card was used for a malicious $10 purchase.
>
> **[8:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=508)** (upbeat instrumental music) This has been a quick introduction to the PCI Data Security Standard.
>
> **[8:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-payment-card-data?u=76281980&t=517)** There's much more detail provided by the PCI Council on this and their other standards, and these are available for download from their website, shown here.

#### Clouding the issues
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Cloud technology is no longer a novel approach to deploying infrastructure, but is a mainstream option for enterprises.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=8)** Cloud security solutions may be deployed by the enterprise IT team, or solutions may be deployed by business groups.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=17)** There are three forms of Cloud deployments in common use, infrastructure as a service, platform as a service and software as a service.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=27)** However, there are many more specialist forms of Cloud service that can be used.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=33)** In all cases, there's a need for security controls to be used to protect the Cloud solution, just as there is for an open premise solution.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=42)** However, there are some differences in the controls when using them for Cloud, and there are some new controls that need to be considered.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=51)** The International Standards Organization has produced an ISO 27,000 standard for Cloud known as ISO 27017.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=61)** This is based on ISO 27002, and includes an additional six controls.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=68)** NIST has produced the SP 800-144, "Guidelines on Security and Privacy in Public Cloud Computing," which refers back to the SP 853 controls.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=81)** However, the main reference for Cloud security is the "Cloud Security Alliance Security Guidance" for critical areas of focus in Cloud computing, with its 14 areas of controls.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=94)** These domains provide a full description of Cloud, its security needs, and the controls needed to protect Cloud deployments.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=103)** The guidance is supported by a Cloud controls matrix, which can be downloaded from the CSA site, shown here.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=111)** The Cloud Controls Matrix provides the fundamental security principles that should be adopted by Cloud vendors and that can assist Cloud customers in assessing the overall security risk of a Cloud provider.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=125)** It provides clarity on the shared responsibilities between the Cloud service provider and customer, according to the form of Cloud.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=134)** More importantly, it provides a controls framework cross-reference to the other major security standards recognized in industry.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=143)** Version 3.0.1 of the CCM has 133 controls in 16 domains.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=151)** The 16 domains can be seen here.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=154)** They don't align with the domains in the security guidance for critical areas of focus in Cloud computing, but they do provide a comprehensive coverage of security across Cloud, starting with application and interface security and finishing with threatened vulnerability management.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/clouding-the-issues?u=76281980&t=172)** However, the main reference for Cloud security is the "Cloud Security Alliance Security Guidance" for critical areas of focus in Cloud computing, with its 14 areas of controls.

#### Securing things on the internet
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The Internet of Things is a term which means everything that's connected to the internet that isn't a standard laptop, workstation or server.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=10)** One dictionary definition of the Internet of Things is the interconnection via the internet of computing devices embedded in everyday objects, enabling them to send and receive data.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=23)** Wikipedia goes a little deeper and defines the Internet of Things as a system of interrelated computing devices, mechanical and digital machines, objects, animals, or people that are provided with unique identifiers and the ability to transfer data over a network without requiring human to human or human to computer interaction.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=46)** An obvious characteristic of the Internet of Things is that it's connected to the internet.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=53)** It may only send data, it may only receive data, or it may do both.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=60)** An important class of internet things are the low power things, those objects which have an embedded battery and no external power supply.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=69)** These are often required to have a life of 10 years or longer, and so require very low power operation.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=78)** One of the first organizations to provide guidance on security for the Internet of Things was the IoT Alliance Australia, a part of the Australian Communications Alliance.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=92)** Its initial Internet of Things Security Guideline was published in February, 2017, and provides an introduction to IoT technology and the key IoT industry sectors.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=105)** It covers legal, privacy, security, resilience, and survivability issues, as well as IoT device development considerations.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=119)** There is no definitive set of security controls for IoT, although organizations such as OWASP and GSMA have provided some guidance.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=129)** The IoT Security Foundation has published a comprehensive set of 142 controls in their security guideline which are grouped into 13 areas of compliance.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=142)** Take a moment to think about the challenges in providing guidance for IoT.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=147)** A small sensor may have little memory and a very low power processor but an industrial SCADA device may be as powerful as a modern PC.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=157)** Think about an IoT soil moisture sensor which is deployed out in the field and has to run in its own internal battery for no less than 10 years.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=167)** Jot down two reasons why you wouldn't want it to have to run antivirus software.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=173)** An interesting additional attribute that the IoT Security Foundation has tagged to each control is its compliance class, which can be one of five values relating to the data generated or the level of control provided by the device.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=193)** The control is then relevant to the IoT device if it's compliance class is equal or higher to the control tag.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=200)** Class 0 means that the compromise is likely to result in little discernible impact on an individual or organization.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=208)** Class 1 means that the compromise would likely have limited impact on an individual or organization.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=216)** Class 2 devices are those designed to resist attacks on availability that would have a significant impact on individuals or an organization.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=227)** Class 3 devices additionally are designed to protect sensitive data and Class 4 devices are those which have the potential to affect critical infrastructure or cause personal injury.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/securing-things-on-the-internet?u=76281980&t=240)** We're likely to see much more attention being given to IoT security controls as we see deployments into key sectors such as intelligent transport and smart cities.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Definitions:** means that (2), is a  (1)
> **Code Keywords:** require (1), class, (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (2)
> **Prerequisites:** required to (1)

#### Affordable cybersecurity

>
