#### Ensuring security is effective
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] While we may design and implement security into our systems, this doesn't mean that it's effective.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=6)** In fact, most breaches occur on solutions which have been supposedly secured using good industry practices.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=14)** The reason for the security failures vary.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=17)** Some breaches occur because of poor design or implementation.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=21)** Some occur because of poor security hygiene in the business and some because new exploits have been found, which enable an attacker to circumvent security.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=32)** There are a variety of basic assurance methods that are used by organizations to check their systems.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=38)** The key ones are vulnerability scans, which identify the services which can be accessed, and check that they have all known patches applied.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=48)** Security control audits, which predefine a set of conditions based on the control and predict the expected results.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=56)** And penetration tests, which use known exploits and exploit techniques to try to circumvent the security control.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=64)** In addition to their own testing, organizations may contract out independent evaluation of their systems.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=71)** This is typically done to provide some form of certification that the system can be trusted by its users.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=78)** Many organizations now gain certification against ISO 27000 for the whole business to demonstrate that their processes covering design, implementation, and operation of systems are secure.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=93)** Vendors may submit their products for evaluation in accordance with what's known as the Common Criteria scheme.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=100)** This provides an independent view of product assurance from a basic statement of document compliance to an increasingly rigorous level of evaluation.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=110)** The Common Criteria scheme is recognized by many countries and is the main scheme used for product assurance.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=117)** However, some governments have begun to question the efficacy of evaluations of overseas products even when they're done locally.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=126)** The original five members of the Common Criteria group were the US, the UK, Australia, Canada, and New Zealand.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=134)** New Zealand and the United Kingdom are no longer authorizing countries.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=139)** Assurance isn't a perfect answer.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=141)** Certified organizations do get breached and evaluated products do get exploited.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=147)** However, carrying out assurance testing raises the bar substantially and avoids organizations falling victim to simple attacks.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/making-sure-security-is-effective?u=76281980&t=155)** With the move to automated assurance testing, that bar is raised even further as the vagaries of the human element are removed from the testing process.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** iso (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Protecting privacy with cybersecurity
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Instructor] The general data protection regulation of the European Union is a law which enforces the protection of the privacy of European citizens, with mandatory rules for how organizations and companies must use any information, which directly or indirectly could identify a living person.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=21)** This is known as personal data, and a couple of simple examples are your name and phone number.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=28)** The actors become globally relevant because it applies to any services being consumed in the EU no matter where they're sourced.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=37)** With a 1% global revenue fine for failure to comply, compliance is a high priority.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=44)** There are six principles in the act.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=46)** Personal data is to be processed in a lawful, fair, and transparent manner.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=52)** These process characteristics don't involve cybersecurity directly.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=57)** It must be collected for a specific purpose and only used for that purpose.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=62)** Again, this is a process characteristic and doesn't involve cybersecurity.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=67)** It must be limited to what's needed for the purpose and no more, another non cybersecurity characteristic.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=76)** It must be accurate and kept up to date.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=79)** Cybersecurity capabilities can be used to maintain data integrity, which contributes to its accuracy.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=86)** But keeping it up to date isn't a cybersecurity capability.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=90)** It must be kept in a form which minimizes the exposure of a person's identity and only retained for as long as necessary.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=99)** Cybersecurity can certainly help here by enabling encryption of data.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=104)** The last principle is fully cybersecurity.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=107)** It requires appropriate security for personal data, including protection against an authorized or unlawful processing, and against accidental loss, destruction, or damage.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=120)** GDPR requires organizations to implement appropriate, technical, and organizational measures to secure personal data.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=130)** This requires businesses to ensure that their cybersecurity defenses continue to be effective against evolving cyber threats.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=139)** A good GDPR compliance program involves risk assessments, ongoing maintenance of the security infrastructure, and continuous monitoring of networks in order to detect and respond to any intrusions.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=155)** Some key cybersecurity controls for GDPR compliance include encryption of personal data, control of access to data, and regular audits.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=166)** These are all standard controls used in most cybersecurity programs.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=170)** An alternative to encryption of data is to mask or tokenize it, and this is a security control generally associated with privacy.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=180)** In addition to the normal controls, a data privacy program needs to have breach notification protocols in the event of a data breach.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=189)** Other privacy specific controls include data minimization practices, data protection impact assessments, and implementing privacy by design principles.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/protecting-privacy-with-cybersecurity?u=76281980&t=201)** While not cybersecurity controls as search, these will often fall to the security team to manage as part of the wider security and privacy program.

> **Env Vars:** gdpr (3)

#### Understanding the zero trust approach to network access
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The term Zero Trust is the new ABC of security, assume nothing, believe no one, and check everything.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=8)** It means that the security mechanisms that we use to protect our systems shouldn't make trust assumptions, but rather should assume that any access request could be malicious.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=18)** The context for Zero Trust is the decision-making process which mediates access from a source or subject, which could be a person, a system, a serverless application or function, or an artificial intelligence to a resource or object, which could be data, a system, an application, or function, or another artificial intelligence.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=39)** There are many definitions of Zero Trust.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=42)** Here, we see that NIST defines Zero Trust as an evolving set of cybersecurity paradigms that move defenses from static network-based perimeters to focus on users, assets, and resources.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=55)** Microsoft has a slightly longer explanation rather than definition of Zero Trust.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=60)** However, we can see that the key sentence here is that every access request is fully authenticated, authorized, and encrypted before granting access.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=69)** CrowdStrike considers Zero Trust to be a security concept that requires all users, even those inside the organization's enterprise network to be authenticated, authorized with a continuously validated security configuration and posture before being granted or keeping access to applications and data.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=88)** The concept of Zero Trust first emerged from Forrester in 2011 in a report by John Kindervag.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=97)** The Forrester concept of Zero Trust is based on two principles.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=101)** First, threats are present at all times, no matter where our system is located.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=106)** We must therefore be prepared to defend against them at all times.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=110)** Second, just because a network is an internal network doesn't make it trusted.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=115)** Our adversaries have shown that they're very capable of penetrating into our networks and moving through them.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=121)** So Zero Trust then is a paradigm which recognizes that a business' secrets are no longer secure behind the corporate perimeter, as they cannot be protected effectively by just having perimeter firewalls.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=134)** While there's no commonly accepted definition for Zero Trust, there are four security mechanisms that are recognized as necessary for a Zero Trust solution.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=143)** Just-in-time access is a mechanism used to provide the means to positively authenticate an entity request at the time the request is made rather than assuming the request is authentic because of a token or certificate that was issued in the past.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=158)** Just enough access is a mechanism which ensures that only those privileges needed to carry out the request are provided and only for the duration of the request.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=167)** This is the approach used by the more advanced privileged access managers, where a new set of credentials are issued just for the duration of a specific work ticket.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=176)** This is a powerful defense against account-based privilege escalation.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=180)** Zero Trust brings with it a notion of data-centric security.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=184)** As such, tokenization or encryption mechanisms are needed to desensitize data and so reduce the data attack surface.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=192)** This means that much of the risk, particularly around data breach, is avoided.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=196)** And the final mechanism is real-time access policy, making dynamic policy adjustments using a rich source of environment information.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/understanding-the-zero-trust-approach-to-network-access?u=76281980&t=205)** This enables access decisions to adapt to a more dynamic environment in which subject's rights and privileges and object sensitivities may change quickly.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** is a  (4), means that (2), is an  (1)
> **Code Keywords:** function (2), static (1), protected (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Env Vars:** abc (1), nist (1)
> **Tools:** notion (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Resilience as an emerging approach
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Instructor] The number of successful attacks on and technical failures of critical infrastructure has been a concern for governments for some time.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=10)** While nations have focused on the full spectrum of their own critical infrastructures, the European Union has focused its efforts on the European financial sector.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=21)** The result has been the enactment of the Digital Operational Resilience Act, or DORA for short.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=29)** In order to achieve a high common level of digital operational resilience across the European financial sector, DORA establishes a common set of security requirements, including cyber risk management, cyber incidents, resilience testing, threat and vulnerability, intelligence sharing and national cooperation, and third-party risk management.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=55)** Risk management under DORA reflects existing best practice in cyber risk management.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=62)** It requires a risk management body to be established with overall responsibility for the risk strategy, setting risk policy, and defining roles and responsibilities.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=75)** This body would approve such things as business continuity, incident response plans, third party services, and audits.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=84)** Organizations are required to support risk management with a comprehensive set of information security policies.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=93)** A documented risk management framework must be put in place, reviewed annually, and be included in the audit plan.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=102)** The framework must specify the organization's risk tolerance and define key performance indicators for information security.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=111)** Continuous risk assessment of business processes and their related technology must be carried out.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=119)** Organizations should establish business continuity plans, which include business impact assessments, and they must regularly test their plans.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=128)** Central security depositories must have a geographically separated secondary processing site for enhanced resilience.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=137)** Business continuity is one of the key aspects of the operational resilience focus of DORA.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=144)** Organizations should have a comprehensive incident management process and document how cyber incidents are detected, the plan for communicating about incidents, and they must provide evidence that this approach is effective.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=161)** Incident management should include early warning indicators.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=165)** Incidents should be prioritized and their response tracked, and root cause analysis should be carried out.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=174)** DORA introduces a classification approach for incidents, which takes into account key aspects such as the duration of the incident, the criticality of the asset, and the economic impact of the incident.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=188)** Major incidents will need to be reported to national and European authorities in a harmonized format.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=195)** Ensuring effective and time response to incidents is another key focus in DORA.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=202)** Organizations are required to perform vulnerability assessments before any technology deployment into the financial infrastructure, and then maintain a comprehensive program of ongoing resilience testing.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=215)** This will include vulnerability scans, analysis of any open source solutions which are in place, assessment of system network and physical security, source code reviews, scenario based and end-to-end testing, and compatibility and performance testing.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=235)** DORA introduces the concept of an advanced Threat-Led Penetration Test, or TLPT, which must be performed by accredited TLPT testers at least once every three years.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=249)** Testers will be expected to demonstrate specific expertise in utilizing threat intelligence and be competent in using penetration and red team testing techniques.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=262)** Organizations must establish a comprehensive register of information detailing contractual arrangements with third-parties.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=270)** Third-party arrangements will be reported annually to national authorities, and in turn, to European authorities.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=279)** Service providers will be required to provide access for inspections and audits.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=286)** Audits will assess various aspects of the service provider's security posture, including data protection, incident response capabilities, disaster recovery plans, and overall operational resilience.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=301)** Cloud providers in particular must demonstrate adherence to DORA regulations regarding risk management, incident reporting, and digital operational resilience testing.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=313)** DORA notes that financial entities may exchange threat intelligence within the trusted financial community.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=320)** In addition, the European authorities will foster cooperation with non-European nations to promote the principles of DORA.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=330)** DORA is a new act for the financial sector, but mostly just reflects existing best practices in information security, but with greater emphasis on areas of resilience.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=344)** Technical standards to provide common levels of protection are being developed and will be issued as delegated regulations for the EU.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/resilience-as-an-emerging-approach?u=76281980&t=353)** These will apply to both financial institutions and their service providers.

#### Ensuring supply chain security through SBOMs
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] There's been an increasing level of concern in governments about the potential for cyber attacks through the supply chain.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=8)** A particular issue is the risk of malicious changes to open source libraries used to build software.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=15)** As a result, the 2021 executive order on improving cybersecurity directed that vendors will provide details of the software components used in their solutions.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=28)** The order directs vendors to provide a purchaser, a software bill of materials, or SBOM, for each product directly, or by publishing it on a public website.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=40)** Pursuant to this requirement, the Department of Commerce issued a publication entitled The Minimum Elements for a Software Bill of Materials.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=49)** This publication covers three areas, the data fields required in an SBOM, support for automating SBOM management, the practices and processes relating to SBOMs.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=64)** The data fields in the SBOM must contain sufficient information about each component to enable purchasers to track them across the software supply chain.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=74)** The SBOM may also include other non-mandatory identifiers.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=79)** Specifically, the baseline component information includes the vendor or supplier name, the component name, the component version, other identifiers such as vulnerability and license database keys, dependencies with other components, and an author and timestamp for the SBOM entry.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=103)** Vendors must provide support for automation so that SBOM management can be scaled across the software ecosystem.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=111)** Tooling of SBOM data management requires well-defined data formats and API specifications.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=117)** There are currently three main SBOM data formats, which are both machine and human readable, and these are interoperable, the Software Package Data Exchange or SPDX formats, CycloneDX, and the Software Identification.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=137)** The third area of guidance is in respect of practices and processes relating to SBOM use.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=143)** These include a new SBOM must be produced to accompany a new release of the component, all top level component details together with their dependencies must be provided to enable lower level dependencies to be found recursively.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=159)** Any unknown component dependencies must be detailed.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=164)** SBOMs should be available in a timely fashion, either by accompanying the software or being accessible, for example, on the vendor website.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=174)** And where a vendor wishes to control access to their SBOM data, accommodation must be made for purchasers to gain access to and use that data.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=183)** Beyond the minimum elements, vendors are encouraged to provide additional information such as references to component entries, invulnerability databases, and license conditions.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=196)** Providing assurance information such as a hash value of the component is also recommended.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=202)** The way in which component vulnerabilities in software as a service is managed is different to that of on-premise products.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/ensuring-supply-chain-security-through-sboms?u=76281980&t=210)** The vendor must assert that they have defined and will manage their SBOM in line with the minimum element guidance.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** sbom (13), api (1), spdx (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (3), for example (1)
> **Code Keywords:** public (1), assert (1)
> **Best Practices:** recommended (1)
> **Speakers:** - [narrator] (1)


### 4. Detecting and Managing Cyber Incidents

#### Incident management basics
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] With the resources being invested in both cybercrime and state-sponsored malware, it's inevitable that an attack will eventually penetrate even the most careful organization.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=12)** When that happens, the difference between inconvenience and disaster will be how well-prepared the organization is to respond to the incident.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=22)** NIST Cybersecurity Framework provides a set of control objectives under the functional area, Respond.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=29)** This consists of five categories: Planning, Communicate, Analysis, Mitigation, and Improvements.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=42)** The framework also includes a recovery function, which adds to the three of the Respond categories.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=49)** The five cybersecurity framework categories align closely with the four-stage incident handling process defined in the NIST Special Publication SP 800-61, Incident Handling Guide.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=64)** Unlike the cybersecurity framework, the communications which occur throughout these four stages is not shown as a separate stage.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=72)** The cybersecurity framework and the SP 800-61 can also be aligned to the three-stage model published by Crest UK, with its model of Prepare, Respond, and Follow Up.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=86)** Whatever the model, a key aspect of incident management is information sharing.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=92)** This includes threat intelligence in the preparation stage and operational response matters during an incident.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=100)** NIST established the Forum of Incident Response and Security Teams, or FIRST, in 1990, and this continues today as an active forum helping support the industry, government and vendor communities.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=115)** FIRST runs workshops and conferences to foster cooperation and coordination in incident prevention to stimulate rapid reaction to incidents and where subject matter experts can meet to share information.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=131)** The Community of Computer Incident Response Teams or CERTs, operate at a national level to protect the government and its critical infrastructure and to provide community advice on cybersecurity matters.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=145)** The US-CERT, for example, is part of the Department of Homeland Security.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=150)** Through its 24-by-7 operations center.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=153)** US-CERT accepts, triages, and collaborates on incidents, provides technical assistance and disseminates notifications of current and potential issues.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=166)** CERTs also collaborate at the international level through the Forum of Incident Response Teams.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=172)** This involves not only maintaining national CERT-to-CERT channels, running training courses, and participating in annual conferences, but also being the main contact for CERTs to organizations such as the Global Forum of Security Experts and the International Telecommunications Union.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=195)** It's useful to have a common language when talking about types of incidents and having a set of generic templates which are fit for purpose for each.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=205)** US-CERT defines seven categories of incidents.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=210)** Category 0 covers incidents that are part of cyber exercises for testing network defenses.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=217)** Category 1 incidents are those where an individual gains logical or physical access without permission to a network system, application, data, or other resource.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=230)** Category 2 incidents are denial-of-service events where the attack successfully prevents or impairs the normal authorized functionality of a network, system, or application by exhausting resources.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=245)** Category 3 covers the successful installation of malicious software, not quarantined by antivirus software.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=254)** Category 4 incidents are those involving a breach of acceptable use.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=259)** Category 5 incidents are scans and probes of a system, looking for open ports, protocols, or services, which don't directly result in a compromise or denial of service.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=273)** Category 6 is for incidents involving unconfirmed, but potentially malicious activity, which justifies further investigation.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=286)** Incidents don't often appear in a way which is immediately obvious for categorization.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=292)** We'll usually have some form of event that's flagged as suspicious and some investigation is needed.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=300)** An important tool for incident management is the trouble ticket system, which enables us to maintain all relevant information on an event through to it becoming an incident and eventually being resolved.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=313)** Here's an example of a trouble ticket system called osTicket, displaying its list of open tickets.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=321)** The US Cybersecurity and Infrastructure Agency runs the National Initiative for Cybersecurity Careers and Studies, and through that, has published what is known as the NICE Framework, which describes workforce roles in cybersecurity.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=338)** There are three roles related to incident response.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=342)** Cyber defense analyst, whose role includes running vulnerability scans, monitoring for attacks, and analyzing malware.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=351)** Cyber defense incident responder, whose role is to investigate, analyze, and respond to cyber incidents, and cyber defense forensics analyst whose role is to analyze digital evidence and investigate incidents.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=367)** The NICE Framework provides a useful reference to the skills and knowledge required for each of these roles.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/incident-management-basics?u=76281980&t=375)** Why don't you pause the course and take a moment to check out the skills and knowledge required to be a cyber defense incident responder, and check out the tasks you'll be expected to undertake.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** cert (5), nist (3), first (2), nice (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), such as (1)
> **Code Keywords:** function (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** osticket (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Measuring incident management maturity
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] The effort put into preparing for an incident will be paid back many times over through a timely and effective response which contains the damage.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=10)** Preparation involves establishing and training an incident response team, establishing and exercising processes, and acquiring the necessary tools and resources.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=21)** Once a basic program is up and running, it's useful to carry out a baseline survey of incident response preparedness.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=29)** CREST UK has developed an incident response maturity assessment tool, which is free to download and use.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=36)** This is a spreadsheet-based tool which contains over 600 questions across the three stages of incident management.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=43)** And it can be used to assess an organization's readiness to respond to a cyber attack.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=49)** A summary version of the tool with just a handful of higher level questions is also available for download from the website shown here.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=58)** Another early task for the incident response team will be to take advantage of the strategic threat intelligence sources which are being used to inform the cyber risk management team.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=69)** In addition to the threat reports, it's useful to have tactical and operational threat intelligence.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=76)** Tactical information exchange is usually available within an operating community.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=83)** A good example of this is the financial services FS-ISAC.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=90)** FS-ISAC is an intelligence sharing community for the banking industry.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=94)** This allows organizations to get early warnings and real-time information about the kind of activities that are impacting other members of the community.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=104)** At an operational level, the use of mechanisms for distributing indicators of compromise provides real-time actionable intelligence for feeding into firewalls and intrusion detection devices.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=118)** MITRE has been leading the development of standards for operational feed mechanisms, and the STIX/TAXII protocols are widely recognized within the incident response community.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=130)** Incident response procedures need to be defined and installed.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=135)** An issue tracking system is an important tool to enable effective incident management from operational detection through to resolution and recovery.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=145)** While a standard service management or IT operations ticket system may include incident tracking, it may not satisfy the full requirements for security incident handling.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=158)** TheHive is an open source cybersecurity incident management system which runs in the cloud and allows multiple teams to collaborate on incident investigations.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=168)** It enables automated analysis at scale of incoming incident information and includes integrated real-time threat intelligence.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=179)** Another requirement is to establish a set of response playbooks which detail the actions to be taken for specific categories of incident.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=189)** Many incident response teams create a jump kit, which is a portable case that contains materials that may be needed during an investigation.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=198)** A jump kit typically includes a laptop loaded with networking and forensic software, backup devices, blank media, and basic networking equipment and cables.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=211)** The preparation stage is a good time to build relationships in the incident response community, so that access to information and support comes naturally during a crisis.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=222)** It's also a good time to build relationships inside the company, particularly with the IT team, so that there's no political stumbling blocks when a response is necessary.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=233)** Finally, incident responders will need to be able to function effectively when managing the containment of an incident.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=241)** And this means having pre-authorization to take unilateral action and make or direct emergency changes.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=249)** The last thing a good crisis needs is decision making by committee.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=255)** With a team established, the key element of ongoing preparation is cyber crisis exercises.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=262)** These exercise the incident response procedures as well as the skills of the team, and provide visibility of the impact of a cybersecurity incident on the organization.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=275)** The initial website provides a substantial amount of training and exercise material which can be used for internal cert training and as the basis for customization to the wider crisis management program.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/measuring-incident-management-maturity?u=76281980&t=288)** This includes handbooks tools, and a full program of pre-exercise training through to complete exercises.

#### Detecting an attack
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's look at the operational response phases of incident response.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=5)** In the NIST model of incident response these are detection and analysis, containment, eradication, and recovery, and post-incident activities.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=17)** Detection and analysis is the non-stop process of monitoring for evidence of a cyber attack, and this is the job of the SOC analyst.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=27)** During the detection phase, the SOC analyst is looking for evidence of malware or intrusive behavior coming into the organization from external sources.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=38)** This will usually involve watching real-time alerting screens, which run 24-by-7.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=44)** The analyst is also looking for evidence of malware that has succeeded in penetrating the organization by running file scans and monitoring for signals going out to the malware's command and control servers.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=58)** A further requirement is to monitor for lateral malicious movement between systems inside the organization to detect malware or an intruder that's penetrating deeper into our networks.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=71)** Here's an example of the monitoring screen which SOC analysts use.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=76)** This is the Splunk system, but there are others, such as Graylog and ELK Stack, each with its own pros and cons.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=83)** All of them, however, digest log records and raise alerts when certain conditions are met.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=90)** Life in the operations room monitoring for attack isn't easy.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=93)** It involves many hours of staring at screens of scrolling log records and alerts for candidate incidents which are relevant to pull out and investigate further.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=106)** Even when there's real evidence of an incident, such as a crashed server, it's often difficult to determine whether the incident is just an IT issue or whether it really is security related, and if so the type, extent, and magnitude of the problem.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=122)** Picking cyber attacks often requires as much intuition as intelligence.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=128)** Another challenge is that many alert sources such as IDS have a high rate of false positives.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=134)** Being under-responsive will let the attack in, but being over-responsive means there's a risk of crying wolf.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=143)** When an incident is confirmed as being security-related, incident responders will often be asked to analyze ambiguous, contradictory, and incomplete symptoms to determine what's happened.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=156)** This is where an analyst's skill really becomes important.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=160)** Signs of an incident fall into one of two categories.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=164)** A precursor is a sign that an incident may occur in the future.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=168)** A port scan may be a precursor to an attack, as an adversary would likely do surveillance before launching a hard attack.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=177)** Similarly, the release of an exploit in the wild to attack a known vulnerability in the organization would be a precursor to an attack.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=188)** An indicator is a sign that an incident may have occurred or may be occurring now.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=194)** A beaconing connection back to an unusual IP address may be an indicator that malware is attempting to make a command and control connection.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=203)** Many of the alerts which are raised in the operations room will be false positives, and it's important to validate any detection before raising alarms.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=212)** Understanding normal behavior is one of the best ways of discriminating between false precursors and indicators and real ones.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=220)** Having a knowledge base helps, as this can be used to quickly determine whether the same anomaly has been seen before.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=229)** Here's what a traffic flow monitoring screen looks like.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=233)** With this, a SOC analyst can check for unusual flows of information such as might occur in a major data breach.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=240)** And here's another showing unusually large amounts of traffic going to a port which normally has minimal traffic flows.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=249)** Detection may involve correlating information over a period of time.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=253)** Today's analytical tools tend to use big data analytics as a key strategy to detect long and slow APT infections.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=262)** Deep packet inspection can be used to provide a detailed snapshot of activity on a particular part of the network, and this may give more context to the precursor or indicator.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=274)** Host-based packet capture tools such as Wireshark can be used, as can network-based devices such as FireEye and NetWitness.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=284)** Once an indicator is turned into an incident, prioritization is perhaps the most critical decision point in the incident handling process.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/detecting-an-attack?u=76281980&t=293)** Incidents shouldn't be handled on a first come, first serve basis, but should be prioritized based on the criticality to the business.

#### Hunting for threats
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Stock analysts don't have to just wait for an attack to be detected.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980&t=4)** They can be proactive and hunt for any threats that might have got into the system undetected.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980&t=10)** Threat hunting involves both looking for the threat agent itself or by detecting traces of activity related to the threat agent.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980&t=18)** For example, finding a file of user credit cards in a temporary shared folder or finding an account which shouldn't exist are both evidence that there may have been an attack.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980&t=30)** A comprehensive set of threat characteristics, what are known as indicators of compromise, are necessary to enable the threat hunter to search for known threats.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980&t=42)** We can also use advanced analytics and big datasets to look for traces of threat activity in logs.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980&t=48)** This is how we might find beaconing, the regular connections malware sends out to its command and control system.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980&t=57)** The threat hunting process is a continuous process of looking around for a trigger to provide the context for a specific investigation, the investigation itself, and then resolution through taking action to mitigate the threat that has been found.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980&t=74)** Idaho Labs, in conjunction with the Department of Homeland Security, has released an excellent tool for threat hunting called Malcolm.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980&t=84)** This tool can be used in real time to monitor an attack as it happens, or more usually, as a way of analyzing a packet capture file to hunt for signs of an attack.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980&t=96)** We can view a dashboard, or we can view the packet capture, either as packets using the Malcolm component called Arkime, or at the session level using the Malcolm component called Zeek.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/hunting-for-threats?u=76281980&t=113)** You can find out more about how to install and run Malcolm in my Kali Purple course.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** find (2)
> **Definitions:** known as (1), is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Prerequisites:** install (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Responding to an incident
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Early containment is necessary to stop an incident overwhelming resources, or increasing the level of damage it inflicts.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=8)** Pre-authorization to take action enables containment and allows time to develop a tailored remediation strategy.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=16)** Containment decisions, such as disconnecting a system, are much easier to make if a response plan template for this kind of incident has been predetermined.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=26)** Separate containment strategies for each major incident type need to be prepared and pre-authorized.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=33)** Most incidents will require an ongoing investigation to trace back to the source and the cause of the attack, and this will occur in parallel with containment and recovery activities.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=45)** This is likely to be the primary role for cyber instant responders, with IT and networks taking the lead on containment and recovery.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=54)** Access to a wide range of sensor information is important to getting the network visibility that's required to fit all the pieces together.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=63)** If the incident is serious, then it's likely that a major incident management event will be called.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=69)** This will typically be run by IT or network operations and will be under the control of the MIM manager.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=77)** A MIM consists of a group of key stakeholders establishing regular meetings or conference calls to monitor the progress of incident resolution, make decisions collaboratively and coordinate messaging.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=92)** Although the primary reason for gathering evidence during an incident is to resolve the incident, it may also be needed for legal proceedings.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=100)** In such cases, it's important to clearly document how all evidence, including compromised systems, has been preserved, using an official chain of custody evidence tag.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=112)** After an incident has been contained, eradication may be necessary to delete malware, disabled breached user accounts and identify and mitigate all vulnerabilities that were exploited.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=125)** It's important to eradicate the issues, not only on the affected hosts, but on all hosts that could be affected through the same or a similar attack.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=134)** For example, removing a default administrator account on one server, whilst leaving the same account open on another, is just asking for more trouble.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=144)** The last and probably most important rule when responding to an incident, is to continue monitoring for other incidents.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/cybersecurity-foundations-22006082/responding-to-an-incident?u=76281980&t=151)** An attack may well be a diversion in order to gain more subtle access somewhere else on the network.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Analogies:** such as (1), for example (1)
> **Exercise Files:** template (1)
